\interfootnotelinepenalty=10000 % force footnotes to appear on the same page

\begin{document}

\title{The Most Efficient Protocol for PAKE: \\
\medskip
\large{What Exact Stuff Do You Need to Hash at the End?}
\thanks{The \texttt{.tex} code of this paper is available at \url{https://github.com/jiayux123/oeke}. \\ Some minor \LaTeX\;hacks, such as lines 21--25 and 29--31, might be useful to beginners.}
}

\author{Jiayu Xu\footnote{Oregon State University, \texttt{xujiay@oregonstate.edu}}}
\date{}

\maketitle
\begin{abstract}
A Password-Authenticated Key Exchange (PAKE) protocol allows two parties to jointly establish a cryptographic session key, in the ``password-only'' setting where the only information shared in advance is a low-entropy password. In recent years, the One-encryption EKE with 2-round Feistel cipher (OEKE-2F) protocol, a compiler from Key Encapsulation Mechanism (KEM) to PAKE, has received much attention, for the following reasons: (1) When instantiated with the Diffie--Hellman KEM, it is the most computationally efficient PAKE scheme to date that is secure in the Universal Composability (UC) framework; and (2) When instantiated with a post-quantum KEM, it provides a generic way to construct efficient PAKE protocols based on post-quantum assumptions.

Unfortunately, the community cannot agree upon what the OEKE-2F protocol exactly is: part of the second protocol message is an RO hash of the KEM key, together with any number of the following:
\begin{itemize}
  \item The password,
  \item The KEM public key,
  \item The first protocol message, and
  \item The KEM ciphertext.
\end{itemize}
This yields 16 potential variants of OEKE-2F; only two of them have been studied in the literature, and the pros and cons of each version are poorly understood.

In this work, we present a comprehensive analysis of \emph{all 16 variants} of OEKE-2F, proving the UC-security of each of them. The general takeaway is that the ``hash everything'' version requires the fewest security properties of the underlying KEM scheme, and the more items we remove from the hash, the more security requirements the KEM scheme has to satisfy --- although all of the additional KEM properties are still mild. We pinpoint the exact KEM properties each version of OEKE-2F needs, and thoroughly explain the rationales.

The significance of this work lies in that it helps the community converge upon the ``right'' version of OEKE-2F, and perhaps also in that this is the first paper by the author that is over 100 pages.
\end{abstract}

\newpage\tableofcontents\newpage

\section{Introduction}
\label{sec:intro}
Ever since the seminal work by Bellovin and Merritt in 1992 \cite{SP:BelMer92}, \emph{Password-Authenticated Key Exchange (PAKE)} has been an enduring topic in cryptography. Such a protocol allows two parties to jointly establish a cryptographically strong session key in the so-called ``password-only'' setting, where the only information shared in advance is a low-entropy password; crucially, it must be secure in the presence of an MitM adversary that can modify protocol messages in an arbitrary manner. PAKE has received much attention from both theoretical and applied communities: theoretically speaking, it achieves something that might appear impossible --- bootstrapping the entropy of a shared secret in such a way that is secure against the strongest network adversary --- and recent years have witnessed some efforts on the standardization and deployment of PAKE protocols in practice \cite{pake-selection}.

\paragraph{Encrypted Key Exchange (EKE).}
Apart from introducing PAKE the concept, \cite{SP:BelMer92} also proposed a highly influential PAKE protocol called \emph{Encrypted Key Exchange (EKE)}. This is a general compiler from any Key Encapsulation Mechanism (KEM) scheme\footnote{EKE is often described using the terms of key exchange protocols, but a 2-message-flow key exchange is equivalent to a KEM (see, e.g., \cite[Section~2.2]{EC:SanGuJar23}).} to a PAKE, and its idea is simple: the two protocol parties (symmetrically) encrypt the KEM public key and ciphertext under their password, respectively, and send the result as protocol messages; holding the same password allows them to decrypt the protocol messages and complete the underlying KEM scheme, which yields the shared KEM key; finally, the two parties output an RO hash of the KEM key as their PAKE session key. 
\begin{figure}[H]
\pseudocodeblock{
P(\pw) \< \< P'(\pw) \\ 
(pk,sk) \gets \Gen \< \< \\
\phi \gets \mathcal{E}(\pw,pk) \< \< \\
\< \sendmessageright*{\phi} \< \\
\< \< pk := \mathcal{D}(\pw,\phi) \\
\< \< (c,K) \gets \Encap(pk) \\
\< \< \phi' \gets \mathcal{E}(\pw,c) \\
\< \sendmessageleft*{\phi'} \< \\
c := \mathcal{D}(\pw,\phi') \< \< \\
K := \Decap(sk,c) \< \< \\
\text{output }SK := \Hkey(K) \< \< \text{output }SK := \Hkey(K)
}
\caption{The EKE protocol with KEM scheme $(\Gen,\Encap,\Decap)$ and symmetric encryption $(\mathcal{E},\mathcal{D})$}
\end{figure}
EKE is almost as efficient as the underlying KEM scheme, with one exception: both parties need to perform an encryption of the KEM public key/ciphertext. For a long time, this encryption mechanism is generally modeled as an Ideal Cipher (IC), which allows the security proof for EKE to go through \cite{EC:BelPoiRog00}. While IC over bitstrings can be implemented using (say) AES, EKE requires an IC \emph{over the KEM public key/ciphertext space}, e.g., a cyclic group if we use the Diffie--Hellman KEM, and it is unclear how to instantiate such an IC efficiently. A standard result \cite{C:DaiSte16} shows that an IC is indifferentiable from an 8-round Feistel cipher, assuming that the underlying round function is an RO; however, applying this IC construction to EKE would mean an overhead of 4 hash-to-group operations for both protocol parties, rendering the efficiency of EKE not ideal.

\paragraph{One-encryption EKE (OEKE).}
Over the years, there have been multiple efforts aimed at improving the efficiency of EKE. The first among them is the variant called \emph{One-encryption EKE (OEKE)}, proposed by Bresson, Chevassut and Pointcheval in 2003 \cite{CCS:BreChePoi03}. Here, the first protocol message is exactly the same as in EKE, but second protocol message is replaced by the ``raw'' KEM ciphertext together with an authenticator defined as an RO hash of the KEM key. In this way, we remove the evaluation of IC in one direction.
\begin{figure}[H]
\pseudocodeblock{
P(\pw) \< \< P'(\pw) \\ 
(pk,sk) \gets \Gen \< \< \\
\phi \gets \mathcal{E}(\pw,pk) \< \< \\
\< \sendmessageright*{\phi} \< \\
\< \< pk := \mathcal{D}(\pw,\phi) \\
\< \< (c,K) \gets \Encap(pk) \\
\< \< \tau' := \Htag(K) \\
\< \sendmessageleft*{c,\tau'} \< \\
K := \Decap(sk,c) \< \< \\
\tau := \Htag(K) \< \< \\
\text{if }\tau = \tau' \text{ output }SK := \Hkey(K) \< \< \text{output }SK := \Hkey(K) \\
\text{otherwise output }SK := \bot \< \<
}
\caption{The OEKE protocol with KEM scheme $(\Gen,\Encap,\Decap)$ and symmetric encryption $(\mathcal{E},\mathcal{D})$}
\label{fig:oeke}
\end{figure}
Apart from being more computationally efficient than EKE, OEKE also achieves one-side \emph{explicit authentication}: the initiator ($P$ in \Cref{fig:oeke}) can check if its counterparty holds the correct password by computing its own authenticator and comparing it with the incoming message; if the check fails, it can explicitly reject.\footnote{It is unclear whether this should be called ``initiator-side explicit authentication'' or ``respondent-side explicit authentication'': It is the initiator who may explicitly reject, but what's checked is whether the respondent holds the correct password. Below we use the term ``initiator-side explicit authentication''.}

\paragraph{Using 2-round Feistel cipher (2F) in place of IC.}
In 2020, an orthogonal optimization of EKE was proposed by McQuoid, Rosulek and Roy \cite{CCS:McQRosRoy20}. Their trick is simple: While a 2-round Feistel cipher (2F) is distinguishable from an IC, it nevertheless can be used in place of IC in EKE. In more detail, they use the following procedure
\begin{align*}
& \underline{\mathsf{2F}(\pw,pk)}: \\
& r \gets \{0,1\}^{3n}, \\
& R := H_1(\pw,r), &T &:= pk \cdot R, \\
& t := H_2(\pw,T), &s &:= r \oplus t, \\
& \text{output }\phi = (s,T)
\end{align*}
instead of IC encryption $\mathcal{E}(\pw,pk)$, and the following procedure
\begin{align*}
& \underline{\mathsf{2F}^{\text{-}1}(\pw,\phi = (s,T))}: \\
& t := H_2(\pw,T), &r &:= s \oplus t, \\
& R := H_1(\pw,r), &pk &:= T/R, \\
& \text{output }pk
\end{align*}
instead of IC decryption $\mathcal{D}(\pw,\phi)$. This mechanism can be viewed as a weaker, randomized variant of IC, which \cite{CCS:McQRosRoy20} calls Programmable-Once Public Function (POPF). \cite{CCS:McQRosRoy20} showed the security of EKE with 2F,\footnote{The security proof in \cite{CCS:McQRosRoy20} is flawed, which was later fixed in \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25}.} whose computational overhead is one hash-to-group operation on each side ($H_1$ above; note that $H_2$ is onto bitstrings) as opposed to four.

\paragraph{OEKE-2F.}
Interestingly, \cite{CCS:McQRosRoy20} did not mention OEKE, and an immediate future direction was to replace the (single) IC in OEKE with 2F. This was the subject of two works in 2025, one by Januzelli, Roy and Xu \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} and the other by Arriaga, Barbosa and Jarecki \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}. While the two works drastically differ in the presentation of security proofs, both showed that OEKE with 2F (henceforth OEKE-2F) is secure in the Universal Composability (UC) framework \cite{FOCS:Canetti01}, the current security standard for PAKE. The computational overhead of OEKE-2F is only one hash-to-group operation \emph{on one side}; indeed, when instantiated with the Diffie--Hellman KEM, this is the most computationally efficient PAKE protocol that has ever been proven UC-secure.\footnote{Three other PAKE protocols, AuthA \cite{PKC:BreChePoi04}, SPAKE2 \cite{RSA:AbdPoi05} and CPace \cite{TCHES:BjoBen19}, have computational costs comparable to that of OEKE-2F (a concrete comparison is difficult, since the tradeoff is between hash-to-group operations and multiexponentiations in the group, and thus depends on the implementation details). However, all of these three protocols are unlikely to be UC-secure (see \cite[Section~1]{C:ABBJKX20} for a discussion). SPAKE2 and CPace do not achieve one-side explicit authentication. All three protocols use a Diffie--Hellman group and are not generic KEM-to-PAKE compilers.}

Apart from its computational efficiency, OEKE-2F has received much attention for two other reasons:
\begin{itemize}
  \item Since OEKE-2F is a generic compiler from \emph{any} KEM scheme (with certain reasonable security properties) to PAKE, plugging in a post-quantum KEM scheme immediately yields a PAKE protocol secure under post-quantum assumptions.
  \item A recent variant of OEKE-2F called Tempo \cite{TCHES:ArrBarJar26} is resilient to timing attacks, while mostly retaining the efficiency of OEKE-2F.
\end{itemize}
\begin{remark}
We caution that ``PAKE secure under post-quantum assumptions'' and ``post-quantum PAKE'' are two different concepts: the latter additionally requires a security proof in the Quantum-accessible ROM (QROM), and such a proof for (O)EKE --- even in the game-based setting --- appears difficult \cite{EPRINT:HHKR25}.
\end{remark}

\paragraph{What exact stuff do you need to hash at the end?}
The above appears to be the happy ending of the whole story, and the reader might wonder why I wrote this (super long) paper at all. The reason is simple: \emph{The OEKE-2F protocols analyzed in \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} and \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} are different!} Looking at their respective protocol descriptions (\cite[Figure~19]{EC:JanRoyXu25} and \cite[Fig.4]{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}), one can realize that the authenticator $\tau'$ and the session key $SK$ are derived differently in these two works: in \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} they are $\Htag(K)$ and $\Hkey(K)$, i.e., exactly as in \Cref{fig:oeke}, whereas in \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} they are $\Htag(\pw,pk,s,T,c,K)$ and $\Hkey(\pw,pk,s,T,c,K)$, i.e., the hash of the KEM key $K$ together with
\begin{itemize}
  \item The password $\pw$,
  \item The KEM public key $pk$,
  \item The first protocol message (i.e., the 2F ciphertext) $\phi = (s,T)$, and
  \item The KEM ciphertext $c$.
\end{itemize}

The two protocols analyzed in \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25,EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} are two extremes on a spectrum of OEKE variants: For each of the four items above, one could choose to include or exclude it in the ROs, resulting in $2^4 = 16$ versions of OEKE. By examining the literature on OEKE, an immediate impression is that \textbf{people simply cannot agree upon what the OEKE protocol exactly is}. Below we list all existing security analyses of OEKE, and what exact items are hashed.
\begin{table}[H]\centering
\begin{tabular}{ |c|c|c|c|c| }
\hline
paper & protocol name & items hashed & \makecell{encryption \\ mechanism} & security notion \\
\hline
\cite{CCS:BreChePoi03} & OEKE & $pk,c$ & IC & game-based \\
\hline
\cite{EC:SanGuJar23} & EKE-KEM & $pk$ & half IC & UC \\
\hline
\cite{ACNS:BCPRR23} & OCAKE & $\pw,\phi,c$ & IC & UC \\
\hline
\cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} & OEKE & & 2F & UC \\
\hline
\cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} & NoIC & $\pw,pk,\phi,c$ & 2F & UC \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{Existing works on OEKE. The KEM key $K$ is always hashed and omitted from the table. We omit the machine-aided analysis in \cite{CSF:Blanchet12} and the post-quantum analysis in \cite{EPRINT:HHKR25}, as they are not the focus of this work}
\label{table:timeline}
\end{table}
This is a confusing, and frankly embarrassing, situation: everyone gives their own OEKE security analysis with some items included in the hash, which is a seemingly arbitrary choice by the authors; it has never been studied --- or even sufficiently explained in an informal manner --- what exact items need to be included, and \emph{why}. In particular, for the state-of-the-art OEKE-2F, only the ``hash everything'' and ``hash nothing but $K$'' versions have been analyzed, and it is unclear whether, or why, those items need to be included in the hash. (Another confusing point is that everyone has their own name for OEKE.)

\paragraph{Why not simply hash everything?}
An immediate question is whether this topic matters at all --- why not simply hash everything like what \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} did? This question, in fact, has been partially answered in \cite[Section~5]{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}, and below we summarize our understanding:
\begin{itemize}
  \item Since the initiator's algorithm has two stages, of course \emph{some} state has to be memorized in between. From \Cref{fig:oeke} it is clear that the KEM secret key $sk$ has to be part of the state. However, the more items we include in the hash, the more the initiator needs to memorize. Therefore, hashing fewer items is beneficial in the setting where the initiator's memory is extremely limited.
  \item Including the password $\pw$ in the hash is especially problematic, since if the initiator's memory is stolen between its two stages, then $\pw$ is immediately leaked. In other words, removing $\pw$ from the hash helps defend against side-channel attacks better. The same goes for the KEM public key $pk$ (note that $pk$ is supposed to be secret in OEKE). In contrast, the first protocol message $\phi$ and the KEM ciphertext $c$ are supposed to be seen by the adversary anyway, so they are not a concern in this regard.
  \item Having a longer hash input means more calls to the underlying compression function while computing the hash, so hashing fewer items improves the computational efficiency (admittedly the bottleneck of computational cost is the KEM and the hash-to-group operations, and the optimization here is minor).
\end{itemize}
These reasons aside, perhaps the primary motivation of this work is that
\begin{displayquote}
  \emph{The current state-of-the-art, where the community cannot converge upon a ``right'' version of OEKE and everyone does an ad hoc analysis, is just sad, especially given that (1) OEKE has been around for over 20 years, and (2) it is one of the most significant PAKE protocols as explained earlier.}
\end{displayquote}
Furthermore, we believe the topic we study is of theoretical interest: The requirements on the underlying KEM scheme depend on the items included in the hash, and it is very interesting to see what exact additional KEM properties are needed when we remove certain items from the hash. Also, the security proofs require careful considerations of the order of games and how the reductions can go through.

\paragraph{Why is this paper so long?}
A common complaint about PAKE papers is that they tend to be long and hard to understand, especially if they prove UC-security. I claim that part of it is due to the nature of PAKE and/or UC, and cannot be circumvented. The main reason is that there are a number of factors to consider, including
\begin{itemize}
  \item Whether the MitM adversary forwards or modifies a protocol message;
  \item If the adversary modifies a protocol message, whether the adversarial message contains a correct password guess, an incorrect password guess, or no password guess; and
  \item Whether the two honest parties' passwords match.
\end{itemize}
Combined, they yield a large amount of cases that need to be covered in the security proof. For example, there has to be (at least) one game showing that if the adversary sends a message that contains an incorrect password guess, then the receiving party's session key is indistinguishable from random; another game has to show that if the adversary is passive (i.e., forwards both protocol messages without modification) and the two honest parties' passwords are different, then the two parties output independently random session keys. (This assumes implicit authentication; in explicit authentication the party(ies) should output the rejection symbol instead of a real session key.)

Still, there are other reasons specific to this paper. One is, of course, that there are too many OEKE variants to analyze. The reason why the introduction and preliminaries (\Cref{sec:prelim}) sections of this paper are unusually long is the following: I wish to explain this work to such an extent that by reading \Cref{sec:intro,sec:prelim} alone, one could gain an intermediate-level understanding of the technical details --- including why certain KEM properties are needed in certain OEKE variants --- and you probably don't need to read the remaining sections. In this way, the ``effective length'' of this paper is roughly 25 pages.

\subsection{Motivating Example: OEKE-2F with Diffie--Hellman KEM}
\label{sec:oeke-dh}
One might ask why removing certain items from the hash requires the underlying KEM scheme to have some additional security properties. To see the subtleties, let's work out a concrete example: OEKE-2F with the Diffie--Hellman KEM scheme. We show the ``hash nothing but $K$'' version below.
\begin{figure}[H]
\pseudocodeblock{
P(\pw) \< \< P'(\pw) \\ 
x \gets \Z_q, X := g^x \< \< \\
(s,T) \gets \mathsf{2F}(\pw,X) \< \< \\
\< \sendmessageright*{s,T} \< \\
\< \< X := \mathsf{2F}^{\text{-}1}(\pw,(s,T)) \\
\< \< y \gets \Z_q, Y := g^y \\
\< \< K := X^y \\
\< \< \tau' := \Htag(K) \\
\< \sendmessageleft*{Y,\tau'} \< \\
K := Y^x \< \< \\
\tau := \Htag(K) \< \< \\
\text{if }\tau = \tau' \text{ output }SK := \Hkey(K) \< \< \text{output }SK := \Hkey(K) \\
\text{otherwise output }SK := \bot \< \<
}
\caption{The OEKE-2F protocol with Diffie--Hellman KEM}
\label{fig:oeke-dh}
\end{figure}
One does not need to understand the UC PAKE functionality --- or \emph{anything} about UC --- to see that this protocol is completely insecure. The adversary $\adv$ disregards $P'$, and sends $(Y := 1, \tau' := \Htag(1))$ to $P$; this will cause $P$'s check to pass, and $P$ will compute its session key as $\Hkey(Y^x) = \Hkey(1)$. In other words, $\adv$ can predict $P$'s session key without knowing $P$'s password --- which shouldn't be allowed by any reasonable security notion of PAKE.

There are three ways to fix this:
\begin{itemize}
  \item If we include $\pw$ in $\Htag$ inputs, i.e., define $\tau'$ as $\Htag(\pw,K)$, then this immediately thwarts the above attack: coming up with a valid $\tau'$ requires $\adv$ to know $\pw$.
  \item Alternatively, we could also include the KEM public key $X$ in $\Htag$ inputs, i.e., define $\tau'$ as $\Htag(X,K)$. Then coming up with a valid $\tau'$ requires $\adv$ to know $X$, which in turn requires $\adv$ to know $\pw$ since the only way for $\adv$ to learn $X$ is to decrypt $(s,T)$ under $\pw$.
  \item Perhaps the most straightforward fix is to disallow the identity group element 1 as a valid protocol message, i.e., $P$ should immediately reject upon receiving $Y = 1$.
\end{itemize}

If we only care about OEKE-2F with Diffie--Hellman KEM, then we are basically done here (it is common practice to exclude 1 in Diffie--Hellman anyway). However, keep in mind that we want to analyze a \emph{generic compiler} from KEM to PAKE, and on this level, the lesson is that
\begin{framed}
\begin{displayquote}
  \emph{If we remove \textbf{both} $\pw$ and $pk$ from $\Htag$ inputs, then the underlying KEM scheme needs to satisfy an additional security property. This property is not needed as long as either $\pw$ or $pk$ is included in $\Htag$ inputs.}
\end{displayquote}
\end{framed}
But what exactly is the additional property? For the specific Diffie--Hellman KEM scheme, this is clear: exclude 1. However, it is surprisingly hard to figure out the required KEM property \emph{in the general case}. Looking ahead, what's needed is \switchocg{name_int}{[click to reveal spoiler]}
\begin{ocg}{name_ext}{name_int}{0}what we call ``collision resistance'', which we define in \Cref{def:cr}\end{ocg}.

The above is only a simple example of the tradeoff between ``what's included in the hash'' and ``what properties the KEM needs to satisfy''; we will see multiple other examples below: removing $c$ from the hash requires an additional KEM property, removing \emph{both} $pk$ and $(s,T)$ from the hash requires another additional KEM property, and so on.
\begin{remark}
The Diffie--Hellman example above is taken from \cite[Section~3.1]{EC:JanRoyXu25}; in particular, \cite[Remark~3.3]{EC:JanRoyXu25} points out that the issue can be fixed by including either $\pw$ or $pk$.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Our Contributions}
As mentioned above, any number of $\pw,pk,(s,T),c$ can be included in $\Htag$ inputs, resulting in 16 variants of OEKE-2F; among them, only the ``hash nothing but $K$'' and ``hash everything'' versions have been previously studied (in \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} and \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}, respectively). This work provides a comprehensive and thorough study of \emph{all 16 variants}, revealing the requirements on the underlying KEM scheme for each of them. Below we summarize our contributions.

\subsubsection{Reanalysis of the ``Hash Everything'' Version}
In \Cref{sec:basic} we analyze the ``hash everything'' version of OEKE-2F, where the authenticator $\tau'$ is defined as $\Htag(\pw,pk,s,T,c,K)$. We choose this protocol as our first target because it is the ``easiest'' one: it requires the fewest properties of the KEM scheme, and it has already been analyzed in \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}.

Of course, the immediate question is why we analyze this protocol again. The answer is that we make multiple improvements over \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}, which we elaborate below.

\paragraph{Improvement \#1: modeling explicit authentication.}
As we have discussed, OEKE has the feature of one-side explicit authentication (1EA): the initiator can tell whether authentication succeeds or not, and output a real session key or the rejection symbol $\bot$ accordingly. This is, unfortunately, not mentioned in \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} (nor in \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25}): if authentication fails, they let the initiator output a uniformly random string as the session key instead of $\bot$. The reason is that the standard UC PAKE functionality is implicit-authentication-only, i.e., it does not allow a protocol party to output $\bot$; thus, the protocol in \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} has to do away with 1EA in order to fit the UC PAKE functionality.

In this work, we propose a UC functionality for PAKE-1EA, and show that the original OEKE-2F protocol --- where the initiator outputs a real session key or $\bot$ --- realizes it. In this way, we prove for the first time in the literature that (any variant of) OEKE satisfies the 1EA property.\footnote{\cite{ACNS:BCPRR23} also gives a PAKE-1EA functionality, but their functionality is problematic and cannot be realized by OEKE (see \Cref{rem:ocake} below).} Our PAKE-1EA functionality only involves a minor modification to the standard PAKE functionality.

\paragraph{Improvement \#2: weaker KEM properties.}
The last paragraph of \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}, titled ``minimum requirements for the KEM'', claims that the ``hash everything'' version of OEKE-2F is UC-secure assuming the underlying KEM scheme has the following three properties:
\begin{itemize}
  \item Pseudorandom public keys (UNI-PK),
  \item One-wayness under one plaintext-checking attack (OW-1PCA), and
  \item Anonymity under one plaintext-checking attack (ANO-1PCA).
\end{itemize}
Close scrutiny shows that both of their definitions of OW-1PCA and ANO-1PCA are too strong, and weaker properties (which we still call OW-1PCA and ANO-1PCA) suffice for OEKE-2F security. Roughly speaking, in OW-1PCA we could disallow the adversary from querying the challenge ciphertext to the plaintext-checking oracle, and in ANO-1PCA we could give the adversary only one public key instead of both. We refer to \Cref{sec:kem-standard} for a formal discussion.

\paragraph{Improvement \#3: fixing flaws.}
We believe the security proof in \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} is mostly correct; in particular, it considers a number of subtleties carefully. However, in the following two cases:
\begin{itemize}
  \item The two parties' passwords are different, and the adversary is passive; and
  \item The two parties' passwords are equal, and the adversary is passive except that it modifies the authenticator $\tau'$,
\end{itemize}
the proof in \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} fails to do a game hop that changes the UC environment's view to the ideal world. We refer to Games 12 and 14 in \Cref{sec:games} for a detailed explanation.

\paragraph{Improvement \#4: more detailed presentation of the security proof.}
Perhaps our most significant improvement over \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} lies in the presentation of the security proof. (The aforementioned three technical improvements are necessary but relatively minor.) The most complicated and subtle step of the proof is the last game (our Game 17 in \Cref{sec:games}, their games $\mathsf{G}_{18}$ and $\mathsf{G}_{19}$ in Section 5); the argument in \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} has a ``reduction within reduction'', i.e., the reduction simulates the game in a manner that is indistinguishable from, but not identical to, the real game, and a second ``inner'' reduction is needed to show this indistinguishability. This complicated proof technique makes their argument hard to follow, and is in fact unnecessary: we show how to make a cleaner argument by defining a separate, tailored KEM property, which is implied by standard properties and which we reduce to in the game.

We list some other improvements on the presentation below:
\begin{itemize}
  \item We drastically simplify the description of the UC simulator by removing unnecessary records.
  \item A few other reductions, including the ones to OW-1PCA and ANO-1PCA, are also quite complicated, and we give detailed descriptions instead of the sketches in \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}. (From our descriptions of these reductions, it is immediately clear why the OW-1PCA and ANO-1PCA properties in \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} are stronger than necessary.)
  \item Our Game 9 in \Cref{sec:games}, which is equivalent to game $\mathsf{G}_{13}$ in \cite[Section~5]{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}, essentially says that the simulator can simulate the $P$-to-$P'$ message $(s,T)$ at random and then program the ROs later if necessary. Our statistical argument why this game hop goes through is much cleaner than theirs.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{Analysis of the Other 15 Variants}
Once we fully work out the ``basic'' security proof for the ``hash everything'' version of OEKE, in \Cref{sec:other-variants-1,app:other-variants-2} we turn to other variants of the protocol. Since the only difference lies in how the $P'$-to-$P$ authenticator $\tau'$ is defined, a large portion of the argument --- e.g., password extraction from the adversarial $P$-to-$P'$ message, and $P'$'s reaction upon receiving this message --- is irrelevant to which OEKE variant we are analyzing and can be reused. However, as seen in \Cref{sec:oeke-dh}, removing certain items from the hash puts more requirements on the KEM scheme, and figuring out these additional properties is highly non-trivial. Below we list the KEM properties needed for all 16 OEKE variants.
\begin{table}[H]\centering
\begin{tabular}{ |c|c|c|c|c|c| }
\hline
\multicolumn{4}{|c|}{items hashed} & \multirow{2}{*}{KEM properties needed} & \multirow{2}{*}{analyzed in...} \\
\cline{1-4}
$\pw$ & $pk$ & $(s,T)$ & $c$ & & \\
\hline
\CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & & \Cref{sec:basic} \\
\hline
\CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & & CBIND & \Cref{sec:hash-pw-pk-st} \\
\hline
\CIRCLE & & \CIRCLE & & CBIND, UNPRE & \Cref{sec:hash-pw-st} \\
\hline
\CIRCLE & & \CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & UNPRE & \Cref{sec:hash-pw-st} \\
\hline
 & \CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & & CBIND & \Cref{sec:hash-pk-st} \\
\hline
 & \CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & & \Cref{sec:hash-pk-st} \\
\hline
 & & \CIRCLE & & CBIND, UNPRE, KBIND, CR & \Cref{sec:hash-st} \\
\hline
 & & \CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & UNPRE, CR & \Cref{sec:hash-st} \\
\hline
\CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & & & CBIND & \Cref{app:hash-pw-pk} \\
\hline
\CIRCLE & \CIRCLE & & \CIRCLE & & \Cref{app:hash-pw-pk} \\
\hline
\CIRCLE & & & & CBIND, UNPRE, KBIND & \Cref{app:hash-pw} \\
\hline
\CIRCLE & & & \CIRCLE & UNPRE, KBIND & \Cref{app:hash-pw} \\
\hline
& \CIRCLE & & & CBIND & \Cref{app:hash-pk} \\
\hline
& \CIRCLE & & \CIRCLE & & \Cref{app:hash-pk} \\
\hline
& & & & CBIND, UNPRE, KBIND, CR & \Cref{app:hash-nothing} \\
\hline
& & & \CIRCLE & UNPRE, KBIND, CR & \Cref{app:hash-nothing} \\
\hline
\multicolumn{6}{|l|}{\makecell{$K$ is always hashed, which is omitted above \\ UNI-PK, OW-1PCA and ANO-1PCA properties are always required}} \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{List of OEKE variants}
\label{table:oeke-variants}
\end{table}
We will explain the various additional KEM properties in \Cref{sec:kem-additional}, but at a high level, they are different types of binding properties. For example, if the KEM ciphertext $c$ is included in the hash, then modifying $c$ but forwarding $\tau'$ will immediately cause $P$ to reject; however, if $c$ is removed from the hash, then we need some additional ``cihpertext binding'' property to prevent the adversary from doing this. We show the full names of the KEM properties below.
\begin{table}[H]\centering
\begin{tabular}{ |c|c|c|c|c|c| }
\hline
name & full name & \makecell{needed if ... is \\ excluded from the hash} & defined in... \\
\hline
UNI-PK & pseudorandom public keys & (needed in all variants) & \Cref{def:uni-pk} \\
\hline
OW-1PCA & \makecell{one-wayness under \\ one plaintext-checking attack} & (needed in all variants) & \Cref{def:ow-1pca} \\
\hline
ANO-1PCA & \makecell{anonymity under \\ one plaintext-checking attack} & (needed in all variants) & \Cref{def:ano-1pca} \\
\hline
CBIND & ciphertext binding & $c$ & \Cref{def:cbind} \\
\hline
UNPRE & unpredictability & $pk$ & \Cref{def:unpre} \\
\hline
KBIND & key binding & $\pw,pk,c$; or $pk,(s,T)$ & \Cref{def:kbind} \\
\hline
CR & collision resistance & $\pw,pk$ & \Cref{def:cr} \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\caption{KEM properties needed for OEKE variants}
\end{table}
The information above could be overwhelming, but the general takeaway is:
\begin{itemize}
  \item In general, the more items we remove from the hash, the more properties are needed for the KEM scheme.
  \item The maximal items that can be removed from the hash ``for free'' are $\pw$ and $(s,T)$. In other words, if $\tau'$ is defined as $\Htag(pk,c,K)$ then no additional KEM properties are needed compared to $\Htag(\pw,pk,s,T,c,K)$.
  \item Removing or not removing $(s,T)$ does not make a difference, \emph{except} that if $pk$ is also removed, then we additionally need KBIND. (This is why we put all variants where $(s,T)$ are removed in the appendices.) 
\end{itemize}

\subsection{How to Read This Paper}
I certainly don't expect anyone to read through all 105 pages of this paper. (Well, perhaps the 2-page table of contents and the 3-page references shouldn't count, but even then it's still 100 pages.) As I said right before \Cref{sec:oeke-dh}, I have organized the paper in such a way that you should gain an intermediate level of understanding of the technical details after reading \Cref{sec:intro,sec:prelim}. In particular, \Cref{sec:kem-standard,sec:kem-additional} explain why certain KEM properties are needed in certain OEKE variants.

Below I discuss who should read further:
\begin{itemize}
  \item If you don't care about all those variants but would like to see a detailed security proof for the basic ``hash everything'' version, read \Cref{sec:basic}.
  \item If, after reading \Cref{sec:basic}, you'd like to additionally see the security proofs for the variants where $\pw$, $pk$ and/or $c$ are removed from the hash, read \Cref{sec:other-variants-1}. Note, however, that I assume familiarity with \Cref{sec:basic} there: I really cannot afford repeating everything in the proof every time I analyze a new variant. I stress again that reading \Cref{sec:prelim} alone should be enough for being convinced that certain OEKE variants need certain KEM properties.
  \item If you'd like to additionally understand the variants where $(s,T)$ are removed from the hash, my suggestion is to read \Cref{app:hash-pw-pk-sim,app:hash-pw-sim,app:hash-pk-sim,app:hash-nothing-sim}. Basically removing $(s,T)$ results in some non-attacks that the UC simulator has to take care of, which are explained in the aforementioned sections;\footnote{Except that if $pk$ is also removed from the hash, you need the KBIND property for the KEM scheme, but that's explained in \Cref{sec:kem-additional}.} the actual security proofs in \Cref{app:game-hops-hash-pw-pk,app:game-hops-hash-pw,app:game-hops-hash-pk,app:game-hops-hash-nothing} are gross and provide little insight.
  \item If your first exposure to the security proof for OEKE was \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} (resp. \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25}), read \Cref{app:comparison} (resp. \Cref{app:jrx-comparison}): they provide high-level comparisons between the security proofs in this paper and in prior works.
  \item I don't know if anyone will be interested in the future directions mentioned in \Cref{sec:future-work}, but if so, then go for it.
\end{itemize}

\section{Preliminaries and Technical Overview}
\label{sec:prelim}
Let $n$ be the security parameter; we assume that all algorithms take $1^n$ as an input, and do not explicitly write it.

\paragraph{Key Encapsulation Mechanisms (KEMs).}
A \emph{Key Encapsulation Mechanism (KEM)} scheme consists of three algorithms:
\begin{itemize}
  \item The \emph{key generation} algorithm $\Gen$ outputs a public/secret key pair $(pk,sk)$. We write $(pk,sk) \gets \Gen$, and use $\pkspace$ to denote the space of the public key $pk$.
  \item The \emph{encapsulation} algorithm $\Encap$ takes as input a public key $pk$, and outputs a ciphertext $c$ and a KEM key $K$. We write $(c,K) \gets \Encap(pk)$.
  \item The (deterministic) \emph{decapsulation} algorithm $\Decap$ takes as input a secret key $sk$ and a ciphertext $c$, and outputs a KEM key $K$. We write $K := \Decap(sk,c)$ or $\Decap(sk,c) = K$.
\end{itemize}

We require two syntactic properties for a KEM scheme:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \emph{Correctness:} We have
      \[
      \operatorname{Pr}
      \left[
      \begin{array}{c}
      K = K'
      \end{array}
      :
      \begin{array}{c}
      (pk,sk) \gets \Gen \\
      (c,K) \gets \Encap(pk) \\
      K' := \Decap(sk,c)
      \end{array}
      \right] \geq 1-\varepsilon_\text{correctness},
      \]
      where $\varepsilon_\text{correctness}$ is negligible. \\
      Note that the probability is taken over the randomness of both $\Gen$ and $\Encap$, i.e., we allow for a negligible portion of ``bad'' $(pk,sk)$ pairs under which correctness holds with low probability.
  \item \emph{High min-entropy:} For any $pk' \in \pkspace$, we have
      \[
      \Pr[pk = pk' : (pk,\star) \gets \Gen] \leq p_\text{guess},
      \]
      where $p_\text{guess}$ is negligible. In other words, a (computationally unbounded) adversary has negligible probability of guessing an honestly generated public key correctly. \\
      The high min-entropy property implies that for $q$ honestly and independently generated public keys, the probability that at least two of them are equal is upper-bounded by ${q \choose 2} \cdot p_\text{guess}$.
\end{itemize}
Whenever we refer to a KEM scheme below, we always assume these two properties and do not explicitly mention them.

The most commonly seen security properties in the KEM literature are indistinguishability (IND-CPA, or just security) and indistinguishability under chosen-ciphertext attacks (IND-CCA, or just CCA), but the KEM scheme in OEKE needs other properties. The ``hash everything'' version of OEKE only requires some relatively standard properties for the underlying KEM scheme, which we define in \Cref{sec:kem-standard}. The variants where certain items are removed from the hash require a number of non-standard properties, which we define in \Cref{sec:kem-additional}.

In all KEM properties below, we use $\Adv_\adv^\text{property}$ to denote the adversary $\adv$'s advantage in the property (either $\adv$'s winning probability in the game, or $\adv$'s distinguishing advantage between two games), and do not repeat it in each definition.

\subsection{KEM Properties Used in the ``Hash Everything'' Version}
\label{sec:kem-standard}
\paragraph{Pseudorandom public keys.}
This property roughly says that an honestly generated public key is indistinguishable from uniformly random.
\begin{definition}
\label{def:uni-pk}
A KEM scheme has \emph{pseudorandom public keys (UNI-PK)} if for any PPT adversary $\adv$, $\Pr[b^* = 1]$ in the following two games are negligibly close:\footnote{The challenge bit $b$ is not actually used in the definition, but it will come in handy in the later security proofs (we sometimes need concise names for these security games).}
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 0$}: \\
    $(pk,\star) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk)$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 1$}: \\
    $pk \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk)$
    }
    \]
\end{definition}
This property is the same as what's called UNI-PK in \cite[Definition~5]{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}. It is needed because otherwise --- as an extreme example, suppose $\Gen$ is so biased that half of the elements in $\pkspace$ (call the set of them $U$) are unreachable by $\Gen$ --- the OEKE adversary $\adv$ can run an offline dictionary attack as follows: $\adv$ tries to decrypt $\mathsf{2F}^{\text{-}1}(\pw^*,(s,T))$ using various $\pw^*$ values, and if the result is in $U$ then $\adv$ knows that $\pw^*$ is not the real password $\pw$. In this way $\adv$ can rule out half of all candidate passwords in expectation.

\paragraph{One-wayness and a variant.}
This property roughly says that given a public key and a ciphertext, an adversary cannot feasibly find the key encapsulated in the ciphertext.
\begin{definition}
A KEM scheme is \emph{one-way (OW)} if for any PPT adversary $\adv$, the probability that $\adv$ wins the following game is negligible:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    $(pk,sk) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(c,K) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $K^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$ \\
    $\adv$ wins if $K^* = K$
    }
\]
\end{definition}
We also need a stronger variant in which the adversary is additionally given access to a \emph{plaintext-checking oracle}, which tells the adversary whether a ciphertext decapsulates to a certain key.
\begin{definition}
\label{def:ow-1pca}
The definition of \emph{one-wayness under one plaintext-checking attack (OW-1PCA)} is the same as that of OW, except that the adversary $\adv$ is additionally given access to an oracle $\PC(sk,\star,\star)$, which on input $(c' \neq c, K')$ outputs 1 if $K' = \Decap(sk,c')$ and 0 otherwise. $\adv$ is allowed to query this oracle only once.
\end{definition}
This property is the same as what's called OW-PCA in \cite[Definition~3]{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}, except that in our notion (1) the adversary can query its plaintext-checking oracle only once, and (2) the adversary is prohibited from querying its plaintext-checking oracle on the challenge ciphertext. The plaintext-checking oracle was first studied in \cite{PKC:AbdBenPoi15} in the context of public-key encryption schemes.

OW-1PCA is needed in the scenario where the OEKE adversary $\adv$ forwards the $P$-to-$P'$ message $(s,T)$ without modification, but then modifies the $P'$-to-$P$ message $(c,\tau')$ to some other $(c^*,\tau^*)$. Consider the time when $P'$ outputs its session key $SK'$: at this point $\adv$ has not done any attack yet, so in the ideal world the PAKE functionality will set $SK'$ at random. This means that $\adv$ must not be able to compute $K'$ (otherwise it can query $\Hkey(K')$ and distinguish $SK'$ from random); furthermore, forward secrecy requires that $\adv$ should not compute $K'$ even if it learns $\pw'$ later (which would allow $\adv$ to compute $pk' := \mathsf{2F}^{\text{-}1}(\pw',(s,T))$) --- which is why we give the public key to the adversary in the security game.

Furthermore, later when $\adv$ modifies $(c,\tau')$ to $(c^* \neq c, \tau^* = \Htag(\dots,K^*))$ for $K^*$ of its choice, it can check if $K^* = \Decap(sk,c^*)$ by observing if $P$'s session key $SK = \Hkey(K^*)$. This corresponds to the (single) query to the plaintext-checking oracle. (This requires that $P$ and $P'$'s passwords match; otherwise $sk$ on the $P$ side does not correspond to $pk'$ on the $P'$ side.)

\paragraph{Anonymity and a variant.}
This property roughly says that given a public key and a ciphertext, an adversary cannot feasibly tell whether the ciphertext corresponds to this public key or some other (freshly generated) public key.
\begin{definition}
A KEM scheme is \emph{anonymous (ANO)} if for any PPT adversary $\adv$, $\Pr[b^* = 1]$ in the following two games are negligibly close:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 0$}: \\
    $(pk,sk) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 1$}: \\
    $(pk,sk) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(pk',\star) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk')$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$
    }
\]
\end{definition}
Similar to OW-1PCA, we also need the 1PCA variant of ANO.
\begin{definition}
\label{def:ano-1pca}
The definition of \emph{anonymity under one plaintext-checking attack (ANO-1PCA)} is the same as that of ANO, except that the adversary $\adv$ is additionally given access to an oracle $\PC(sk,\star,\star)$, which on input $(c' \neq c, K')$ outputs 1 if $K' = \Decap(sk,c')$ and 0 otherwise. $\adv$ is allowed to query this oracle only once.
\end{definition}
This property is the same as what's called ANO-PCA in \cite[Definition~4]{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25}, except that in our notion (1) the adversary can query its plaintext-checking oracle only once, and (2) the adversary is not given $pk'$ (whereas in their notion $pk'$ is generated and given to the adversary in both games).

ANO-1PCA is needed again in the scenario where the OEKE adversary $\adv$ forwards the $P$-to-$P'$ message $(s,T)$ without modification, but then modifies the $P'$-to-$P$ message $(c,\tau')$. Consider $c$: in the real world $c$ is the result of $\Encap(pk)$ (assuming the two parties' passwords match), whereas in the ideal world the simulator does not know $pk$ at this point (it learns no information about $\pw$, so it cannot decrypt $(s,T)$ and learn $pk$) and has to simulate $c$ as the result of $\Encap(pk')$ for a fresh $pk'$. $\adv$ should not be able to tell these two cases apart (even after it learns $\pw$ later and uses it to compute $pk := \mathsf{2F}^{\text{-}1}(\pw,(s,T))$), which is ANO. 1PCA is needed for the same reason as in OW-1PCA.

\paragraph{Anonymity with uniform public keys and random oracles.}
Finally, we define a highly tailored KEM property that is implied by the preceding more standard properties. This property is for facilitating the security proof for OEKE-2F only; we do not expect it to be of independent interest.
\begin{definition}
\label{def:uni-ano-ro}
A KEM scheme is \emph{anonymous with $q$ uniform public keys and random oracles ($q$-UNI-ANO-RO)} if $\Pr[b^* = 1]$ in the following two games are negligibly close:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 0$}: \\
    $pk_1, \dots, pk_q \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $i \gets \adv(pk_1, \dots, pk_q)$ \\
    $(c,K) \gets \Encap(pk_i)$ \\
    $h_1 := H_1(K); h_2 := H_2(K)$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(c,h_1,h_2)$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 1$}: \\
    $pk',pk_1, \dots, pk_q \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $i \gets \adv(pk_1, \dots, pk_q)$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk')$ \\
    $h_1 \gets \{0,1\}^n; h_2 \gets \{0,1\}^{2n}$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(c,h_1,h_2)$
    }
\]
Here, $H_1$ and $H_2$ are ROs with range $\{0,1\}^n$ and $\{0,1\}^{2n}$, respectively.
\end{definition}

This property emerges when the OEKE adversary $\adv$ sends $(s^*,T^*)$ to $P'$ which contains a wrong password guess. In the real world, $(s^*,T^*)$ decrypt to a uniformly random KEM public key, which is then used to compute $c,SK,\tau$ (the $b = 0$ game). In simulation, we want to remove the decryption process and outright use a freshly sampled public key to compute $c,SK,\tau$ (the $b = 1$ game). ($h_1,h_2$ in \Cref{def:uni-ano-ro} correspond to $SK,\tau$ in the protocol, respectively.) What complicates things is that $\adv$ can decrypt a large number of $(s^*,T^*)$ pairs \emph{under both correct and incorrect passwords}, resulting in a large number of $pk^*$ values, \emph{before} sending $(s^*,T^*)$ to $P'$ (or even before initiating $P'$'s session); at this time the reduction does not know which $(s^*,T^*)$ will be used by $\adv$, or which password is correct, yet it has to program the public key (the ``right'' one that will be used in $P'$'s algorithm) into the decryption process. As such, we need to give the reduction polynomially many public keys.

It is not hard to see that $q$-UNI-ANO-RO is implied by the more standard properties:
\begin{lemma}
\label{lem:uni-ano-ro}
Suppose the UNI-PK, OW and ANO properties hold for a KEM scheme. Then for any polynomial $q$, the $q$-UNI-ANO-RO property also holds for the KEM scheme.
\end{lemma}
The proof is deferred to \Cref{app:uni-ano-ro-proof}.

\subsection{Additional KEM Properties Used in Other Versions}
\label{sec:kem-additional}
\paragraph{Ciphertext binding.}
This property roughly says that given an honest tuple of $(pk,c,K)$, the adversary cannot feasibly find another valid $(pk,c^*,K)$ tuple (i.e., a new ciphertext that works for the same public key and the same KEM key). In other words, the public key/KEM key pair $(pk,K)$ binds a ciphertext. This essentially requires that the adversary cannot merely change the \emph{format} of the ciphertext without changing its actual content, which is a very weak property.
\begin{definition}
\label{def:cbind}
A KEM scheme is \emph{ciphertext binding (CBIND)} if for any PPT adversary $\adv$, the probability that $\adv$ wins the following game is negligible:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    $(pk,sk) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(c,K) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $c^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$ \\
    abort if $c^* = c$ \\
    $\adv$ wins if $\Decap(sk,c^*) = K$
    }
\]
\end{definition}
CBIND is needed in OEKE varants where $c$ is removed from $\Htag$ inputs. Then we must prevent the OEKE adversary $\adv$ from forwarding the $P$-to-$P'$ message $(s,T)$ without modification, but then modifying the $P'$-to-$P$ message $(c,\tau')$ to some $(c^*,\tau)$ which makes $P$'s check pass.\footnote{\label{fn:cbind}It is, in fact, unclear whether this is an ``actual'' attack: $\adv$ causes the two parties to output the same session key by changing $c$ and forwarding all other protocol messages, but $\adv$ does not learn the session key or the password. However, at least such an $\adv$ makes the UC simulation fail, since the simulator is not able to tell that $\adv$ is sending a valid $c^*$ (it does not know $sk$). The same comment is true for the attacks in the ``key binding'' paragraph below.} (If $c$ is included in $\Htag$ then $P$'s check will not pass unless $(\dots, c, \dots)$ and $(\dots, c^*, \dots)$ form a collision for $\Htag$.) This should not happen even after $\adv$ later learns $\pw$ and uses it to compute $pk := \mathsf{2F}^{\text{-}1}(\pw,(s,T))$.

\paragraph{Unpredictability.}
This property roughly says that an adversary $\adv$ cannot feasibly output a ciphertext/KEM key pair $(c^*,K^*)$ ``out of thin air'', such that $(pk,c^*,K^*)$ is a valid tuple for an honestly generated public key $pk$ that $\adv$ does not know.
\begin{definition}
\label{def:unpre}
A KEM scheme is \emph{unpredictable (UNPRE)} if for any PPT adversary $\adv$, the probability that $\adv$ wins the following game is negligible:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    $(\star,sk) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(c^*,K^*) \gets \adv()$ \\
    $\adv$ wins if $\Decap(sk,c^*) = K^*$
    }
\]
\end{definition}
While UNPRE is a very weak property, it is not implied by other properties we have seen. For example, a (contrived) KEM scheme could have a ``special'' pair of ciphertext $c^*$ and KEM key $K^*$ such that $\Decap(sk,c^*) = K^*$ for any valid secret key $sk$; this does not necessarily violate any of the standard properties (in particular, OW is not necessarily violated since the probability that $\Encap(pk)$ hits $c^*$ might be negligible).

UNPRE is needed in OEKE varants where $pk$ is removed from $\Htag$ inputs. If the OEKE adversary $\adv$ can come up with $c^*,K^*$ that break the UNPRE property, then it can compute $\tau^* := \Htag(\dots, K^*)$ and send $(c^*,\tau^*)$ to $P$. This causes $P$'s check to pass, and $P$ will output $SK := \Hkey(K^*)$, which $\adv$ can compute. If $\pw$ is also removed from $\Htag$ inputs then $\adv$ can compute $P$'s session key without knowing the password, which clearly breaks the security of OEKE. If $\pw$ is included in $\Htag$ then it seems that $\adv$ is not ``actually'' breaking the security of OEKE, but at least the UC simulator cannot tell that $\adv$ is performing this attack (it does not know $pk$), so it is unclear how the simulator should proceed in this case. (If $pk$ is included in $\Htag$ then the attack above does not work, since $\adv$ cannot compute the correct $\tau^*$ without knowing $pk$.)

\begin{remark}
\cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} seems to have noticed that some form of unpredictability is needed if $pk$ is removed from $\Htag$ inputs. \cite[Section~5]{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} says:
\begin{displayquote}
  \emph{We note that, although it is tempting to remove $pk$ from the $\Htag$ input by arguing that $(\pw,[(s,T)])$ fix a single $pk$ following the IC intuition. However, this gives rise to cases in the proof where the adversary may try to break the protocol by delivering ciphertexts that are valid for all public keys. It is likely that these cases can be excluded by assuming additional properties on the KEM, but we choose not to do so.}
\end{displayquote}
However, it is unclear to us what ``ciphertexts that are valid for all public keys'' exactly means.
\end{remark}

\paragraph{Key binding.}
This property roughly says that given an honest tuple of $(pk',\allowbreak c,K')$ and another honestly generated public key $pk$, an adversary cannot feasibly come up with a ciphertext $c^*$ such that $(pk,c^*,K')$ is also a valid tuple. In other words, the KEM key $K'$ binds a public key/ciphertext pair, although we give the adversary the power of choosing one ciphertext only, and both public keys and the other ciphertext are simply given to the adversary. (In the actual definition, we let $pk'$ be sampled uniformly at random, rather than honestly generated.)
\begin{definition}
\label{def:kbind}
A KEM scheme is \emph{key binding (KBIND)} if for any PPT adversary $\adv$, the probability that $\adv$ wins the following game is negligible:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    $(pk,sk) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $pk' \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,K') \gets \Encap(pk')$ \\
    $c^* \gets \adv(pk,pk',c,K')$ \\
    $\adv$ wins if $\Decap(sk,c^*) = K'$
    }
\]
\end{definition}
KBIND is needed in OEKE variants where $\pw$, $pk$ and $c$ are \emph{all} removed from $\Htag$ inputs. The attack only works in the setting where $P$'s password $\pw$ and $P'$'s password $\pw'$ are different. It is more subtle than previous attacks, so let us describe the OEKE adversary $\adv$ in more detail:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item On protocol message $(s,T)$ from $P$, forward $(s,T)$ to $P'$ without modification. This causes $P'$ to decrypt $pk' := \mathsf{2F}^{\text{-}1}(\pw',(s,T))$, which is uniformly random and independent of $pk$ since $\pw' \neq \pw$. $P'$ then sends $(c,\tau' := \Htag(s,T,K'))$ to $P$ (intercepted by $\adv$) and outputs $SK' := \Htag(K')$.
  \item Suppose $\adv$ now learns $\pw'$ (i.e., after $P'$'s session completes); then $\adv$ can use it to recover $pk'$. If $\adv$ can find another $c^*$ that breaks the KBIND property, then $\adv$ can send $(c^*,\tau')$ to $P$. $P$ will compute the same $K'$ upon decapsulation, so its check $\tau' \stackrel{?}{=} \Htag(s,T,K')$ will pass and $P$ will output the same $\Htag(K')$ as $P'$ does.
\end{enumerate}
Similar to the attack in the ``weak non-malleability'' paragraph, $\adv$ causes the two parties to output the same session key by changing $c$ and forwarding all other protocol messages. (If $\pw$ is included in $\Htag$ then $P$'s check will not pass unless $(\pw, \dots)$ and $(\pw', \dots)$ form a collision for $\Htag$. If $pk$ is included in $\Htag$ then $P$'s check will not pass unless either (1) the uniformly random and independent $pk'$ happens to be equal to $pk$, or (2) $(\dots, pk, \dots)$ and $(\dots, pk', \dots)$ form a collision for $\Htag$. Similarly, if $c$ is included in $\Htag$ then $P$'s check will not pass unless $(\dots, c, \dots)$ and $(\dots, c^*, \dots)$ form a collision for $\Htag$.)

Furthermore, KBIND is also needed if $pk$ and $(s,T)$ are \emph{both} removed from $\Htag$ inputs. The attack goes as follows (here $\pw$ and $\pw'$ need to be the same if $\pw$ is included in $\Htag$):
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Ignore protocol message $(s,T)$ from $P$, and send random junk $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T)$ to $P'$. This causes $P'$ to decrypt $pk' := \mathsf{2F}^{\text{-}1}(\pw,(s^*,T^*))$, which is uniformly random and independent of $pk$. $P'$ then sends $(c,\tau' := \Htag(\pw,K'))$ to $P$ (intercepted by $\adv$) and outputs $SK' := \Htag(K')$.
  \item Suppose $\adv$ now learns $\pw'$ (i.e., after $P'$'s session completes); then $\adv$ can use it to recover $pk'$. If $\adv$ can find another $c^*$ that breaks the KBIND property, then $\adv$ can send $(c^*,\tau')$ to $P$. $P$ will compute the same $K'$ upon decapsulation, so its check $\tau' \stackrel{?}{=} \Htag(\pw,K')$ will pass and $P$ will output the same $\Htag(K')$ as $P'$ does.
\end{enumerate}
In this attack, $\adv$ modifies the $P$-to-$P'$ message $(s,T)$, but still forwards $\tau'$. Again, this poses a problem to the UC simulator since the simulator cannot tell if $\adv$ is sending a valid $c^*$ or not (it does not know $sk$). (If $pk$ is included in $\Htag$ then $P$'s check will not pass unless either (1) the uniformly random and independent $pk'$ happens to be equal to $pk$, or (2) $(\dots, pk, \dots)$ and $(\dots, pk', \dots)$ form a collision for $\Htag$. If $(s,T)$ is included in $\Htag$ then $P$'s check will not pass unless $(\dots, s,T, \dots)$ and $(\dots, s^*,T^*, \dots)$ form a collision for $\Htag$.)

Note that the OEKE adversaries above only know $pk'$ and $c$, whereas in the definition of KBIND we additionally give $pk$ and $K'$ to the adversary. This is because the \emph{reduction} in our OEKE security proof (see \Cref{clm:htag4}) needs $pk$ to simulate the $P$-to-$P'$ message $(s,T)$, and needs $K'$ to simulate $P'$'s output $SK'$. It is possible that a reduction to a weaker KBIND property (where $pk,K'$ are not given the adversary) can go through by sampling $s,T,K'$ at random, but that would require changing the order of the games in the security proof, and we do not explore this possibility.

\medskip
We also consider a much weaker variant of KBIND, where the adversary $\adv$ is forced to output $c^* = c$. Since $\adv$ is passive now, this is in fact a statistical property, and we show that it is implied by UNI-PK and OW:
\begin{lemma}
\label{lem:kbind-eq}
Suppose the UNI-PK and OW properties hold for a KEM scheme. Then
\[
      \operatorname{Pr}
      \left[
      \begin{array}{c}
      K = K'
      \end{array}
      :
      \begin{array}{c}
      (pk,sk) \gets \Gen \\
      pk' \gets \pkspace \\
      (c,K') \gets \Encap(pk') \\
      K := \Decap(sk,c)
      \end{array}
      \right]
\]
is negligible.
\end{lemma}
The proof is straightforward and is deferred to \Cref{app:kbind-eq-proof}.

\Cref{lem:kbind-eq} shows that assuming UNI-PK and OW, we can strengthen KBIND so that $\adv$ is required to output $c^* \neq c$.

\paragraph{Collision resistance.}
This property roughly says that given two honestly generated public keys $pk,pk'$, an adversary cannot feasibly come up with a ciphertext $c^*$ that decapsulates to the same KEM key $K^*$ under the corresponding secret keys $sk,sk'$. In other words, an (adversarially generated) ciphertext/KEM key pair $(c^*,K^*)$ binds an (honestly generated) public/secret key pair.
\begin{definition}
\label{def:cr}
A KEM scheme is \emph{collision resistant (CR)} if for any PPT adversary $\adv$, the probability that $\adv$ wins the following game is negligible:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    $(pk,sk) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(pk',sk') \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(c^*,K^*) \gets \adv(pk,pk')$ \\
    $\adv$ wins if $\Decap(sk,c^*) = \Decap(sk',c^*) = K^*$
    }
\]
\end{definition}
A very similar notion, called strong collision freeness (SCFR-CPA), has been studied in the context of public-key encryption schemes \cite{EC:GruMarPat22}. However, the adversary in SCFR-CPA only needs to output a KEM ciphertext, whereas we additionally require the adversary to output the corresponding KEM key.

CR is needed in OEKE variants where $\pw$ and $pk$ are \emph{both} removed from $\Htag$ inputs. This is to prevent the following attack: The OEKE adversary $\adv$ completely disregards $P'$, and on $(s,T)$ from $P$, $\adv$ chooses two password guesses $\pw_1^*,\pw_2^*$ and computes $pk_1^* := \mathsf{2F}^{\text{-}1}(\pw_1^*,(s,T)), pk_2^* := \mathsf{2F}^{\text{-}1}(\pw_2^*,(s,T))$. If $\adv$ can find $(c^*,K^*)$ that breaks the CR property for $pk_1^*,pk_2^*$, it then sends $(c^*,\tau^* = \Htag(s,T,c^*,K^*))$ to $P$. As long as one of $\pw_1^*,\pw_2^*$ is equal to $P$'s password $\pw$, $P$'s check will pass and $\adv$ can compute $P$'s session key $SK = \Hkey(K^*)$. In this way $\adv$ can make two password guesses per session, which is not allowed by PAKE security. (If either $\pw$ or $pk$ is included in $\Htag$ then the attack above does not work, since $\adv$ needs to commit to a single $(\pw^*,pk^*)$ pair while sending $\tau^*$, unless $\adv$ finds a collision in $\Htag$.)

\medskip
It is unclear whether our new KEM properties are of independent interest. Some of them, such as CR, appear natural enough; while properties such as KBIND look highly tailored (especially since in KBIND one of the public keys is generated honestly, and the other is sampled uniformly at random). Either way, we do not know of any other applications of these properties yet.

\subsection{Example: Diffie--Hellman KEM}
It is instructive to see what cryptographic assumptions the various KEM properties are, in the well-known Diffie--Hellman KEM. This KEM scheme works in a finite cyclic group with (public) generator $g$ and prime order $q$ superpolynomial in $n$, and
\begin{itemize}
  \item $\Gen$ samples $x \gets \mathbb{Z}_q$, computes $X := g^x$, and outputs $(X,x)$.
  \item $\Encap(X)$ samples $y \gets \mathbb{Z}_q$, computes $Y := g^y$ and $K := X^y$, and outputs $(Y,K)$.
  \item $\Decap(x,Y)$ outputs $Y^x$.
\end{itemize}
(Note that the computation of the ciphertext $Y$ does not rely on the public key $X$; in terms of key exchange, Diffie--Hellman is 1-simultaneous round, whereas KEM can model any 2-message-flow key exchange protocol.) The Diffie--Hellman KEM is perfectly correct (i.e., $\varepsilon_\text{correctness} = 0$), and $p_\text{guess} = 1/q$. Furthermore, it satisfies various properties we have defined (below we always assume $x,y \gets \mathbb{Z}_q$):
\begin{itemize}
  \item UNI-PK says that $X = g^x$ is indistinguishable from uniformly random, which holds perfectly.
  \item OW says that given $(X = g^x, Y = g^y)$ it is infeasible to compute $K = g^{xy}$, which is the Computational Diffie--Hellman (CDH) assumption.
  \item OW-1PCA says that given $(X = g^x, Y = g^y)$ and an oracle that on input $(Y' \neq Y, K')$ checks if $(Y')^x = K'$ (which the adversary can query only once), it is infeasible to compute $K = g^{xy}$. This is the Gap Diffie--Hellman (GDH) assumption, except that (1) when the adversary makes a DH oracle query, its first input is fixed to be $X$, (2) when the adversary makes a DH oracle query, its second input must not be equal to $Y$, and (3) the adversary can query the (restricted) DH oracle only once.\footnote{GDH with restriction (1) in place, namely the oracle only takes two inputs $(Y',Z)$ and checks if $Z = (Y')^x$, is sometimes called ``Strong Diffie--Hellman (SDH)'' \cite[Definition~9]{RSA:AbdBelRog01}. Some people (e.g., \cite[Section~2]{CiC:LLMT25}) use the term ``GDH'' to refer to SDH.} This limited version of GDH can be reduced to CDH (on the single oracle query, the reduction to CDH returns 1 with probability 1/2 and 0 with probability 1/2; in this way the reduction has a 1/2 chance of simulating the game correctly).
  \item ANO says that $(X = g^x, Y = g^y)$ and $(X = g^x, Y' = g^{y'})$ for another $y' \gets \mathbb{Z}_q$ are indistinguishable, which holds perfectly. (This property holds perfectly for any KEM scheme where the ciphertext does not rely on the public key.) It of course holds perfectly even if the adversary is additionally given the plaintext-checking oracle.
  \item CBIND says that given $(X = g^x, Y = g^y)$ it is infeasible to come up with $Y^* \neq Y$ such that $(Y^*)^x = X^y$, which holds perfectly.
  \item UNPRE says that it is infeasible to come up with $(Y^*,K^*)$ such that $K^* = (Y^*)^x$, which holds statistically.
  \item KBIND says that given $(X = g^x, X', Y = g^y, (X')^y)$ where $X'$ is a freshly sampled random group element, it is infeasible to come up with $Y^*$ such that $(Y^*)^x = (X')^y$. We claim that this is implied by the CDH assumption. Since $x = 0$ with negligible probability $1/q$, below we assume this does not happen, and the adversary's task is to compute $((X')^y)^\frac{1}{x}$. Let $Z = (X')^y$, and consider a stronger adversary that is additionally given $\log_g Z$. Note that $X$ and $Z$ are just two independent and uniformly random group elements, so computing $Z^\frac{1}{x} = (g^\frac{1}{x})^{\log_g Z}$ is equivalent to computing $g^\frac{1}{x}$. But computing $g^\frac{1}{x}$ given $X = g^x$ is the inverse Diffie--Hellman problem, which is well-known to be equivalent to CDH.\footnote{Inverse Diffie--Hellman is equivalent to square Diffie--Hellman \cite[Section~2.2]{ICICS:BaoDenZhu03}, which in turn is equivalent to CDH \cite[Section~2.1]{ICICS:BaoDenZhu03}.}
  \item CR says that given $(X = g^x, X' = g^{x'})$ for $x' \gets \mathbb{Z}_q$ independent of $x$, it is infeasible to come up with $(Y^*,K^*)$ such that $(Y^*)^x = (Y^*)^{x'} = K^*$. Note that \textbf{this property does NOT hold for the Diffie--Hellman KEM as described above}, since the adversary can simply output $(Y^* = 1, K^* = 1)$. (This is what caused us trouble in \Cref{sec:oeke-dh}.) To fix this, we have to dictate that the identity group element \emph{not} be a valid ciphertext, i.e., $\Decap(\star,1)$ must output $\bot$. Then CR is a statistical property: such $Y^*$ does not exist unless $x = x'$, which happens with negligible probability $1/q$.
\end{itemize}

Overall, we conclude that the Diffie--Hellman KEM has all desired properties under the CDH assumption, \emph{except for CR where we need to exclude $1$ as a valid ciphertext}. Since CR is needed in OEKE variants where both $\pw$ and $pk$ are excluded from $\Htag$ inputs (see \Cref{sec:kem-additional}), this precisely matches what we said in \Cref{sec:oeke-dh}: the ``not hashing $\pw$ or $pk$'' version of OEKE instantiated by Diffie--Hellman (\Cref{fig:oeke-dh}) is insecure, and adding \emph{either} $\pw$ \emph{or} $pk$ fixes this issue.
\begin{remark}
The argument in the CBIND and KBIND paragraphs clearly requires the group order to be prime. The argument in the CR paragraph also makes this requirement, although it can be straightforwardly extended to the general case where the group order has at least one large prime factor (a requirement we have to make anyway, since otherwise discrete logarithm is easy in the group due to the Pohlig--Hellman algorithm). None of the other properties requires the group order to be prime.
\end{remark}

\subsection{The UC PAKE Functionality}
We define the UC PAKE with initiator-side explicit authentication functionality $\Fpake$ in \Cref{fig:pake-func}. Most of it is identical to the standard UC PAKE functionality (with implicit authentication) in \cite{EC:CHKLM05}; the differences are highlighted in {\color{blue}blue}.

\begin{figure}[H]
\begin{framed}
\begin{small}
\begin{itemize}
  \item On input $(\NewSession, sid, P, P', \pw, \text{role})$ from $P$, send $(\NewSession, sid, P,\allowbreak P')$ to $\sim$. Furthermore, if this is the first $\NewSession$ message for $sid$, or this is the second $\NewSession$ message for $sid$ and there is a record $\left<P',P,\star,\star\right>$, then store $\left<P,P',\pw,\text{role}\right>$ and mark it $\fresh$. \quad \textcolor[gray]{0.5}{// $P$'s counterparty is $P'$, its password is $\pw$, and its role is ``role''}
  \item On $(\TestPwd, sid, P, \pw^*)$ from $\sim$, if there is a record $\left<P,P',\pw,\star\right>$ marked $\fresh$, then do:
      \begin{enumerate}
        \item If $\pw^* = \pw$, then mark the record $\compromised$ and send ``correct guess'' to $\sim$.
        \item If $\pw^* \neq \pw$, then mark the record $\interrupted$ and send ``wrong guess'' to $\sim$.
      \end{enumerate}
        \item On $(\NewKey, sid, P, SK^* \in \{0,1\}^n)$ from $\sim$, if there is a record $\left<P,P',\pw,\text{role}\right>$, and this is the first $\NewKey$ message for $sid$ and $P$, then output $(sid, SK)$ to $P$, where $SK$ is defined as follows:
            \begin{enumerate}
              \item If the record is $\compromised$, then set $SK := SK^*$. \quad \textcolor[gray]{0.5}{// Successful online attack. This is the only case where $SK^*$ matters; in the two cases below the functionality ignores $SK^*$ and defines $SK$ independently}
              \item If the record is $\fresh$, a session key $(sid, SK')$ has been output to $P'$, at which time there was a record $\left<P',P,\pw,\text{role}'\right>$ marked $\fresh$, then set $SK := SK'$. \quad \textcolor[gray]{0.5}{// If no attack in this session in either direction, and passwords match, then session keys should also match}
              \item Otherwise, {\color{blue}if $\text{role} = \text{``respondent''}$}, sample $SK \gets \{0,1\}^n$; {\color{blue}if $\text{role} = \text{``initiator''}$, set $SK := \bot$}.
            \end{enumerate}
            Finally, mark the record $\completed$. \quad \textcolor[gray]{0.5}{// Cannot send $\TestPwd$ after instance completes}
    \end{itemize}
\end{small}
\end{framed}
\caption{UC PAKE with initiator-side explicit authentication functionality $\Fpake$. Differences with the standard UC PAKE functionality in \cite{EC:CHKLM05} are highlighted in {\color{blue}blue}}
\label{fig:pake-func}
\end{figure}

We refer the reader to \cite[Section~2.1]{EPRINT:Xu25} for an explanation of the standard UC PAKE functionality; here we only explain the differences (the {\color{blue}blue} text). The two protocol parties are called ``initiator'' and ``respondent'': the initiator sends the first protocol message, which the respondent waits for before it sends its own message. In order to match the OEKE protocol, our functionality achieves explicit authentication for the initiator side only; that is, if the initiator's algorithm does not result in a correct execution, it will output $\bot$ instead of an $n$-bit session key. There are three scenarios of an incorrect execution:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item The MitM adversary performs a failed online attack on the initiator (i.e., the initiator's session record is $\interrupted$).
  \item The adversary does not perform an online attack on the initiator (i.e., the initiator's session record is $\fresh$), but performs a (successful or failed) online attack on its counterparty.
  \item The adversary does not perform an online attack on either party, but the two parties' passwords are different.
\end{enumerate}
All these three possibilities are covered by the third bullet under $\NewKey$,\footnote{The third bullet also covers the possibility that the adversary does not perform an online attack on party $P$ (i.e., $P$'s session record is $\fresh$), and $P$ outputs its session key before its counterparty does. However, in this case $P$ cannot be the initiator, since the initiator outputs after the respondent.} in which case we let the initiator output $\bot$. There is no change from the standard UC PAKE functionality on the respondent side, since the respondent always outputs an $n$-bit session key (i.e., the respondent only performs implicit authentication).
\begin{remark}
\label{rem:ocake}
\cite[Fig.4]{ACNS:BCPRR23} also gives a PAKE-1EA functionality; however, their functionality is problematic and cannot be realized by (any version of) OEKE. The issue is that their functionality treats the three incorrect execution scenarios above differently: in case (1) it lets the initiator output \textsf{error}, in case (3) it lets the initiator output a uniformly random session key, and the paper does not seem to describe what the functionality should do in case (2). In the real-world protocol, the initiator can tell that authentication fails but cannot tell which is the case, so it has to either output a uniformly random session key \emph{in all cases} (which is the standard PAKE functionality) or output $\bot$ \emph{in all cases} (which is the PAKE-1EA functionality).
\end{remark}

\section{UC Description of the OEKE-2F Protocol}
\label{sec:protocol-uc}
We give a formal, UC-compatible description of the OEKE-2F protocol in \Cref{fig:protocol-uc}. Note that UC PAKE considers the case that the two parties' passwords are different; therefore, we need to use different notations $\pw$ and $\pw'$ for the password of $P$ and $P'$, respectively.\footnote{\cite[Fig.4]{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} uses $\pw$ for both parties' passwords, which is not the ``right'' protocol description in the UC setting.} We only describe the ``hash everything'' version of the protocol; in other variants one or more of $\pw$, $pk$, $(s,T)$ and $c$ can be removed from the inputs to $\Htag$. We omit $sid$ from RO inputs (e.g., we write ``$H_1(\pw,r)$'' rather than ``$H_1(sid,\pw,r)$''), although we stress that $sid$ must be included in order to achieve domain separation.

\begin{figure}[H]
\begin{framed}
\begin{small}
Let $\mathcal{G}$ be a PPT algorithm that outputs a (multiplicative) Abelian group $\group$ whose order is superpolynomial, and where uniformly sampling, multiplication and inverse can be done in PPT. (In the case of Diffie--Hellman KEM we need $\group$ to be cyclic and $|\group|$ to be public, but we do not make such requirements in general.) We implicitly assume that $\group \gets \mathcal{G}$ is run as part of the initialization process. Furthermore, we assume that all algorithms take $\group$ as an input, and do not explicitly write it. \\
Let $(\Gen,\Encap,\Decap)$ be a KEM scheme whose public-key space $\pkspace = \group$.

\medskip
\underline{Party $P$, step 1}

\medskip
On input $(\NewSession, sid, P', \pw, \text{``initiator''})$, generate $(pk,sk) \gets \Gen$, sample $r \gets \{0,1\}^{3n}$, compute
\begin{align*}
R &:= H_1(\pw,r), &T &:= pk \cdot R, \\
t &:= H_2(\pw,T), &s &:= r \oplus t,
\end{align*}
and send $(sid,s,T)$ to $P'$. Store $\langle\pw,pk,sk,s,T\rangle$ associated with $sid$.

\medskip
\underline{Party $P'$}

\medskip
On input $(\NewSession, sid, P, \pw', \text{``respondent''})$ and $(sid,s,T)$ from $P$, compute
\begin{align*}
t' &:= H_2(\pw',T), &r' &:= s \oplus t', \\
R' &:= H_1(\pw',r'), &pk' &:= T/R',
\end{align*}
$(c,K') \gets \Encap(pk')$, $SK' := \Hkey(K')$, and $\tau' := \Htag(\pw',pk',s,T,c,K')$, send $(sid,c,\tau')$ to $P$, and output $(sid,SK')$.

\medskip
\underline{Party $P$, step 2}

\medskip
On $(sid,c,\tau')$ from $P'$, retrieve $\langle\pw,pk,sk,s,T\rangle$ associated with $sid$. Then compute $K := \Decap(sk,c)$, $\tau := \Htag(\pw,pk,s,T,c,K)$, and
\[
SK :=
\begin{cases}
\Hkey(K) & \text{if }\tau = \tau' \\
\bot & \text{if }\tau \neq \tau'
\end{cases}
\]
and output $(sid,SK)$. \quad \textcolor[gray]{0.5}{// No need to store $\pw$, $pk$ or $(s,T)$ in step 1 if not included in $\Htag$ (but always need to store $sk$)}
\end{small}
\end{framed}
\caption{UC description of the OEKE-2F protocol (the ``hash everything'' version)}
\label{fig:protocol-uc}
\end{figure}

\paragraph{Correctness.}
Suppose $\pw = \pw'$ and there is no adversary. Then $P'$ computes
\begin{align*}
t' &= H_2(\pw',T) = t, &r' &= s \oplus t' = r, \\
R' &= H_1(\pw',r') = R, &pk' &= T/R' = pk,
\end{align*}
so $(c,K')$ is the result of $\Encap(pk)$ for an honestly generated $pk$. Then on the $P$ side, $P$ computes $K := \Decap(sk,c)$, which is equal to $K'$ except with probability $\varepsilon_\text{correctness}$ (the correctness error of the KEM scheme). If $K = K'$ then $\tau = \tau'$ so $P$'s check passes, and $P$ will output $SK = SK'$. Therefore, the OEKE protocol has correctness error $\varepsilon_\text{correctness}$.

\section{Security Analysis of the ``Hash Everything'' Version}
\label{sec:basic}

The main theorem in this section is:
\begin{theorem}
\label{thm:basic}
Suppose the UNI-PK, OW-1PCA and ANO-1PCA properties hold for the KEM scheme. Then the OEKE-2F protocol as described in \Cref{fig:protocol-uc} UC-realizes $\Fpake$ (\Cref{fig:pake-func}), in the random oracle model for $H_1$, $H_2$, $\Hkey$ and $\Htag$.
\end{theorem}
The rest of this section is dedicated to the proof of \Cref{thm:basic}. In \Cref{sec:simulator} we describe the simulator, and in \Cref{sec:games} we argue why the simulator generates a view indistinguishable from the real-world view.

As standard in UC proofs, w.l.o.g. we only construct a simulator $\sim$ for the ``dummy'' adversary $\adv$ that merely forwards all messages between the environment $\env$ and protocol parties \cite[Claim~11]{FOCS:Canetti01}. We omit $sid$ in protocol messages and honest parties' outputs (e.g., we say ``$P$ outputs $SK$'' rather than ``$P$ outputs $(sid,SK)$'').

\subsection{The Simulator}
\label{sec:simulator}
\subsubsection{Simulation Strategy}
\label{sec:sim-intuition}
A large portion of the simulator is routine; below we explain the idea behind the non-trivial parts.
\paragraph{Simulating honest $P$-to-$P'$ message.}
When $P$'s session begins, $\sim$ must simulate its message $(s,T)$ to $P'$. This can be easily done: just sample $(s,T)$ at random. However, every time $\adv$ queries $H_2(\pw^*,T)$ (let the result be $t^*$), $\sim$ needs to generate a KEM key pair $(pk^*,sk^*)$ for later use (see the ``Extracting password guess from malicious $P'$'' paragraph below) and make sure that $(pk^*,sk^*)$ is consistent with the RO queries. This can be done as follows: $\sim$ computes
\[
r^* := s \oplus t^*, \quad R^* := T/pk^*,
\]
and programs $H_1(\pw^*,r^*) := R^*$. This also allows $\sim$ to store the association between $\pw^*$ and $(pk^*,sk^*)$. (Note that $(pk^*,sk^*)$ needs to be freshly generated every time $\adv$ queries $H_2(\star,T)$; otherwise $R^*$ never changes and $\adv$ can easily find collisions in $H_1$.)
\paragraph{Extracting password guess from malicious $P$.}
When the adversary $\adv$ sends a message $(s^*,T^*)$ to $P'$, if it is different from the honest message $(s,T)$, the simulator $\sim$ must extract a password guess (if any) and send a corresponding $\TestPwd$ command to $\Fpake$. There are two types of attack, which need to be addressed separately:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \emph{``Normal'' online attack:} $\adv$ executes $P$'s algorithm on a password guess $\pw^*$. That is, $\adv$ generates KEM public key $pk^*$, samples randomness $r^*$, computes
      \begin{align*}
      R^* &:= H_1(\pw^*,r^*), &T^* &:= pk^* \cdot R^*, \\
      t^* &:= H_2(\pw^*,T^*), &s^* &:= r^* \oplus t^*,
      \end{align*}
      and sends $(s^*,T^*)$ to $P'$. \\
      It takes a while to realize how to extract $\pw^*$ from $(s^*,T^*)$. First, $\sim$ should scan all $H_2(\star,T^*)$ queries, which yields multiple $\pw^*$ values; $\sim$ must decide which one is the ``actual'' password guess. This can be done via the following. For every $H_2(\pw^*,T^*)$ query (let the result be $t^*$), $\sim$ computes the corresponding $r^* := s^* \oplus t^*$, and checks if (and when) $H_1(\pw^*,r^*)$ was queried. \emph{With overwhelming probability, there is at most one such $H_1$ query made before the $H_2(\pw^*,T^*)$ query}, from which $\sim$ can extract the ``actual'' password guess $\pw^*$.
  \item \emph{Related key attack:} This attack works only after $\adv$ receive an honest $(s,T)$ pair from $P$. $\adv$ skips generating $pk^*$ or sampling $r^*$; instead, $\adv$ picks any $\Delta \in \group$, computes $T^* := T \cdot \Delta$ and
      \begin{align*}
      t^* &:= H_2(\pw^*,T), &(t^*)' &:= H_2(\pw^*,T^*), \\
      \delta &:= t^* \oplus (t')^*, &s^* &:= s \oplus \delta,
      \end{align*}
      and sends $(s^*,T^*)$ to $P'$. \\
      Let us first explain what $\adv$ is trying to achieve here. The same randomness $r$ yields two valid messages, $(s,T)$ and $(s^*,T^*)$, such that
      \[
      s \oplus s^* = H_2(\pw,T) \oplus H_2(\pw,T^*),
      \]
      which allows $\adv$ to pick $T^*$ and ``reverse engineer'' the corresponding $s^*$ if it guesses $\pw$ correctly. $(s^*,T^*)$ implicitly encrypts $pk^* = T^*/H_1(\pw,r)$, which $\adv$ can compute as $pk \cdot (T^*/T)$. \\
      To extract $\pw^*$ from $(s^*,T^*)$, $\sim$ should scan all $H_2(\star,T)$ and $H_2(\star,T^*)$ queries (which yield multiple candidate $\pw^*$ values) and check which ones satisfy
      \[
      H_2(\pw^*,T) \oplus H_2(\pw^*,T^*) = s \oplus s^*.
      \]
