        \item If there is exactly one such query, retrieve record $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$. If there is more than one such record, abort. If there is no such record, send $(\TestPwd, sid, P, \bot)$ and then $(\NewKey, sid, P, 0^n)$ to $\func$.
        \item Compute $K := \Decap(sk^*,c^*)$. If $K^* \neq K$, send $(\TestPwd, sid, P', \bot)$ and then $(\NewKey, sid, P, 0^n)$ to $\func$.
        \item Send $(\TestPwd, sid, P, \pw^*)$ to $\func$.
            \begin{enumerate}
              \item If $\func$ replies with ``wrong guess'', send $(\NewKey, sid, P, 0^n)$ to $\func$.
              \item If $\func$ replies with ``correct guess'', compute $SK := \Hkey(K)$ and send $(\NewKey, sid, P, SK)$ to $\func$.
            \end{enumerate}
      \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}
\end{small}
\end{framed}
\caption{UC simulator $\sim$ for the ``hash $pk$'' variant of OEKE. Only the paragraph that needs essential change from \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-pk-st} (shown in {\color{blue}blue}) is included}
\label{fig:oeke-sim-hash-pk}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection{Changes in Game Hops}
\label{app:game-hops-hash-pk}
Again, we only describe Game 2 and the four new games (there are four new games rather than three, as we need to additionally consider the aborting condition in step 2(a) of \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-pk}).

\medskip\noindent\emph{Game 2:} In the case where $(\pw \neq \pw' \land (s^*,T^*) = (s,T)) \lor c^* \neq c$, find $\adv$'s $\Htag(pk,K^*)$ query resulting in $\tau^*$. If there is none or more than one, set $SK := \bot$. If there is a unique such query, do the following:
\begin{itemize}
  \item If $K^* = K$, set $SK := \Hkey(K)$ (i.e., there is no change from Game 1).
  \item If $K^* \neq K$, set $SK := \bot$.
\end{itemize}

Furthermore, in the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land c^* = c$, do the same as above, except that we now find $\adv$ \emph{or $P'$}'s $\Htag(pk,K^*)$ query resulting in $\tau^*$.

The difference between the new Game 2 and the old one is that we now have two separate cases:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item $(\pw \neq \pw' \land (s^*,T^*) = (s,T)) \lor c^* \neq c$;
  \item $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land c^* = c$.
\end{enumerate}
These two cases combined yield $\neg(\pw = \pw' \land (s^*,T^*) = (s,T) \land c^* = c)$, which is identical to the case in the old Game 2 (i.e., they cover all situations other than the one addressed in Game 1). In case (1) we proceed as in the old Game 2, whereas in case (2) we cannot due to the ``attack'' in \Cref{fig:attack-no-pw}; instead, in case (2) we need to include $P'$'s $\Htag$ query as if it were $\adv$'s.
\begin{claim}
\label{clm:htag7}
Suppose the CBIND property holds for the KEM scheme. In case (1) above, the probability that $P'$ queries $\Htag(pk,K)$ is negligible.
\end{claim}
\begin{proof}
$P'$ makes a single $\Htag$ query, on $(pk',K')$. There are the following cases:
\begin{itemize}
  \item If $(\pw \neq \pw' \land (s^*,T^*) = (s,T))$, then the argument in the proof of \Cref{clm:htag3} still goes through, so $\Pr[pk = pk'] \leq (q_1+1)/|\group|$.
  \item If $c^* \neq c$, we claim that $pk = pk' \land K = K'$ happens with negligible probability. Suppose $pk = pk'$. Then we can reduce the event $K = K'$ to the CBIND property of the KEM scheme, via a reduction $\Adv_{\red_\text{CBIND}}^\text{CBIND}$ similar to that in the proof of \Cref{clm:htag2}. We have $\Pr[pk = pk' \land K = K'] \leq \Adv_{\red_\text{CBIND}}^\text{CBIND}$.
\end{itemize}

If $pk \neq pk'$ or $K \neq K'$ then of course $P'$'s query is not $\Htag(pk,K)$. Overall, we have
\[
\Pr[P'\text{ queries }\Htag(pk,K)] \leq \Adv_{\red_\text{CBIND}}^\text{CBIND} + \frac{q_1+1}{|\group|},
\]
which  yields the claim.
\end{proof}
The remaining argument is essentially identical to that in the old Game 2.

\medskip
Games 3--16 are essentially unchanged from the corresponding games in \Cref{sec:hash-pw-st}.

\medskip\noindent\emph{New game I:} Now that $(\pw')^*$ --- the password guess extracted from $(s^*,T^*)$ --- is defined (in Game 15), we can finally add (part of) what's in step 2 of \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-pk}. In this game we first add the aborting condition. Concretely, in the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (\pw')^* = \pw' \land (c^*,\tau^*) = (c,\tau')$, there must be a $pk'$ defined on the $P'$ side. If there is more than one record $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$, abort. If $(\pw')^*$ is undefined, treat it as if $(\pw')^* \neq \pw'$. (This corresponds to step 2(a) in the simulator in \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-pk}.)

In each $\langle\pw^*,pk^*,\star\rangle$ record, $(pk^*,\star) \gets \Gen$ is freshly generated, so the probability that one of these $pk^*$ happens to be equal to $pk'$ is upper-bounded by $q_2 \cdot p_\text{guess}$ (recall that there is a $\langle\pw^*,pk^*,sk^*\rangle$ record for each $H_2(\pw^*,T)$ query). We have
\[
\Dist_\env^{16,\text{newI}} \leq q_2 \cdot p_\text{guess}.
\]

\medskip\noindent\emph{New game II:} Again in the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (\pw')^* = \pw' \land (c^*,\tau^*) = (c,\tau')$, if there is a unique record $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$, and $\pw^* = \pw$, set $SK := SK'$. (In some sense this is the most crucial new game, as it covers the scenario in \Cref{fig:attack-no-pw} --- which is why we need to make all these changes to begin with. It also corresponds to step 2(b)(ii) in the simulator in \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-pk}. We will deal with step 2(b)(i) later.)

Since $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land c^* = c$, Game 16 finds $\adv$ or $P'$'s $\Htag$ query resulting in $\tau^*$. $P'$ makes a single query to $\Htag$, on input $(pk',K')$; and the query result is $\tau' = \tau^*$. Therefore, Game 16 finds this query, and does the following (see the description of Game 13 in \Cref{sec:hash-pk-st-games}):
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Find the (unique) record $\langle\pw^*,pk',sk'\rangle$.
  \item Check if $\pw^* = \pw$, which is indeed the case.
  \item Compute $K := \Decap(sk',c^*)$ and check if $K = K'$. Steps 1 and 2 above mean that there is a (unique) $\langle\pw,pk',sk'\rangle$ record. But then
      \begin{gather*}
      (c,K') \gets \Encap(pk')\text{ on the }P'\text{ side}, \\
      c^* = c, \\
      K = \Decap(sk',c^*)\text{ on the }P\text{ side},
      \end{gather*}
      and $(pk,sk)$ are an honestly generated KEM public/secret key pair (again, because there is a $\langle\pw,pk',sk'\rangle$ record), so by the correctness of the KEM scheme, the $K' \stackrel{?}{=} K$ check passes except with probability $\varepsilon_\text{correctness}$.
  \item Set $SK := \Hkey(K) (= \Hkey(K') = SK')$.
\end{enumerate}
This entire process in Game 16 eventually results in setting $SK := SK'$ except with probability $\varepsilon_\text{correctness}$, which exactly matches the new game. We have
\[
\Dist_\env^{\text{newI},\text{newII}} \leq \varepsilon_\text{correctness}.
\]

\noindent\emph{New game III:} In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land c^* = c$ (i.e., case (2) in Game 2), we find $\adv$ \emph{or $P'$}'s $\Htag(pk^*,K^*)$ query resulting in $\tau^*$ --- except that in new Game II's condition, new Game II already shortcuts it to $SK := SK'$ without trying to find any $\Htag$ query. In this game, we do not check $P'$'s $\Htag$ query anymore, and only check $\adv$'s $\Htag$ query.

There are the following cases:
\begin{itemize}
  \item If $\tau^* \neq \tau'$, then of course $P'$'s $\Htag$ query does not result in $\tau^*$ (it results in $\tau'$), so we can safely drop $P'$'s $\Htag$ query.
  \item If $\tau^* = \tau'$, but there is more than one $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$ record, then both new Game II and new Game III abort, regardless of $\Htag$ queries by $\adv$ or $P'$. Similarly, if $\tau^* = \tau'$, but there is no $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$ record, then both new Game II and new Game III outright set $SK := \bot$. Thus, the two games are identical.
  \item If $(\pw')^* \neq \pw' \land \tau^* = \tau'$, then $P'$'s $\Htag(pk',K')$ query results in $\tau^*$, and we need to argue that this query can be dropped from the check.
      \begin{itemize}
        \item If $\adv$ queries $\Htag(pk',K')$, then new Game II still finds this query, so new Game II and new Game III are identical.
        \item If $\adv$ does not query $\Htag(pk',K')$, then new Game III sets $SK := \bot$. We argue that new Game II also sets $SK := \bot$ except with negligible probability. New Game II finds $P'$'s $\Htag(pk',K')$ query, then finds the $\langle\pw^*,pk',sk'\rangle$ record, and sets $SK := \bot$ unless $\pw^* = \pw \land \Decap(sk',c^*) = K'$. We claim that $\pw^* = \pw$ with negligible probability; in other words, suppose there is a record $\langle\pw,pk,\star\rangle$, then $pk = pk'$ with negligible probability. This is because $pk$ and $pk'$ are independent of each other by \Cref{clm:pk-ind}, so $\Pr[pk = pk'] \leq p_\text{guess}$.
      \end{itemize}
  \item If $(\pw')^* = \pw' \land \tau^* = \tau'$, there is a unique $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$ record, but $\pw^* \neq \pw$, then again both new Game II and new Game III outright set $SK := \bot$, regardless of $\Htag$ queries by $\adv$ or $P'$. Thus, the two games are identical.
\end{itemize}

The remaining case is that $(\pw')^* = \pw' \land \tau^* = \tau'$, there is a unique $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$ record, and $\pw^* = \pw$. The condition of the game additionally requires $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land c^* = c$. Combined, we get ``$(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (\pw')^* = \pw' \land (c^*,\tau^*) = (c,\tau')$, there is a unique record $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$, and $\pw^* = \pw$''. This is exactly the condition in new Game II, in which case we do not make any change in new Game III. Overall, we conclude that
\[
\Dist_\env^{\text{newII},\text{newIII}} \leq p_\text{guess}.
\]

Game 17 is essentially unchanged from the corresponding game in \Cref{sec:hash-pw-st}.

\medskip\noindent\emph{New Game IV:} In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (c^*,\tau^*) = (c,\allowbreak\tau')$, there is a unique record $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$, and $\pw^* \neq \pw$, set $SK := \bot$. (This corresponds to step 2(c)(i) in \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-pk}.)

In this case Game 17 first tries to find $\adv$'s (unique) $\Htag(pk^*,K^*)$ query resulting in $\tau^*$. Since $\tau^* = \tau' = \Htag(pk',K')$, if $\adv$ does not query $\Htag(pk',K')$, then Game 17 sets $SK := \bot$. Otherwise Game 17 finds $\adv$'s $\Htag(pk',K')$ query, and then tries to find the (unique) $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$ record, and checks if $\pw^* = \pw$ (see Game 13). Since $\pw^* \neq \pw$, the check never passes, so Game 17 again sets $SK := \bot$. Either way, Game 17 matches new Game IV which outright sets $SK := \bot$. We have
\[
\Dist_\env^{17,\text{newIV}} = 0.
\]

\paragraph{Summary.}
We have made the following essential changes to the games:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land c^* = c$, Game 2 tries to find $P'$'s $\Htag$ query resulting in $\tau^*$ (in addition to $\adv$'s query).
  \item In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (\pw')^* = \pw' \land (c^*,\tau^*) = (c,\tau')$, and there is more than one record $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$, new Game I aborts.
  \item In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (c^*,\tau^*) = (c,\tau')$, there is a unique record $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$, and $\pw^* = \pw$, new Game II outright sets $SK := SK'$ (without trying to find any $\Htag$ query by $\adv$ or $P'$).
  \item In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land c^* = c$, new Game III drops the check on $P'$'s $\Htag$ query introduced in Game 2 (except in new Game II's condition, where all checks on $\Htag$ queries have already been dropped).
  \item In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (c^*,\tau^*) = (c,\tau')$, there is a unique record $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$, and $\pw^* \neq \pw$, new Game IV outright sets $SK := \bot$.
\end{enumerate}

Items (1) and (4) introduce the check on $P'$'s $\Htag$ query and then drop it, so there is no net change there. To see the net change, we only need to combine items (2), (3) and (5), which say that in the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (c^*,\tau^*) = (c,\tau')$ and there is a record $\langle\pw^*,pk',\star\rangle$,
\begin{itemize}
  \item If the record is not unique, then the game aborts [item (2)].
  \item If the record is unique, and $\pw^* = \pw$, then we set $SK := SK'$ [item (3)].
  \item If the record is unique, and $\pw^* \neq \pw$, then we set $SK := \bot$ [item (5)].
\end{itemize}
This exactly matches step 2 of the simulator in \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-pk}, so new Game IV is identical to the ideal world. This completes the proof.

\paragraph{The ``hash $pk$ and $c$'' version.}
If we define $\tau$ as $\Htag(pk,c,K)$, then \Cref{clm:htag7} unconditionally holds, and the protocol can be proven secure without the CBIND property of the KEM scheme (i.e., under the same assumptions as in the ``hash everything'' version).

\subsection{The ``Hash Nothing But $K$'' Version}
\label{app:hash-nothing}

The last variant we consider is that both $\pw$ and $pk$ (as well as $c$) are removed from $\Htag$ inputs, i.e., $\tau$ is defined as the ``bare'' $\Htag(K)$. There is an even more general ``attack'' against this version that the simulator must take into consideration, which we start from.

\subsubsection{Changes to the Simulator}
\label{app:hash-nothing-sim}
We show the new situation in \Cref{fig:attack-no-pw-pk}, using the specific OEKE with Diffie--Hellman KEM.

\begin{figure}[H]
\pseudocodeblock[codesize=\scriptsize]{
P(\pw) \< \< \adv \< \< P'(\pw') \\ 
\< \sendmessageright*[1.5cm]{s,T} \< \< \< \\
\< \< X := \mathsf{2F}^{\text{-}1}(\pw,(s,T)) \< \< \\
\< \< (s^*,T^*) \gets \mathsf{2F}(\pw',X^2) \< \< \\
\< \< \<\sendmessageright*[1.5cm]{s^*,T^*} \< \\
\< \sendmessageleft*[1.5cm]{Y^2,\tau'} \< \< \sendmessageleft*[1.5cm]{Y,\tau'} \< \< \\
\text{output} \< \< \< \< \text{output} \\
\Hkey((Y^2)^x) = \Hkey(g^{2xy}) \< \< \< \< \Hkey((X^2)^y) = \Hkey(g^{2xy})
}
\caption{New ``attack'' on the ``hash $\pw$'' version, using OEKE based on Diffie--Hellman KEM}
\label{fig:attack-no-pw-pk}
\end{figure}

In this ``attack'', the two parties' passwords may or may not be the same. Assuming $\adv$ knows both, it can decrypt $P$’s message to recover $X = g^x$, and then re-encrypt $X^2$ under $\pw'$; this causes $P'$ to output $SK' = \Hkey(g^{2xy})$ and send $(Y = g^y, \tau' = \Htag(g^{2xy}))$. Then $\adv$ sends $(Y^2,\tau')$ to $P$, causing $P$'s check to pass and $P$ will also output $\Hkey(g^{2xy})$. This is a generalization of both ``attacks'' in \Cref{app:hash-pw} (where $\adv$ can change $pk$ but not $\pw$ during re-encryption) and \Cref{app:hash-pk} (where $\adv$ can change $\pw$ but not $pk$ during re-encryption); here, since neither $\pw$ nor $pk$ is included in $\Htag$ inputs, $\adv$ can change \emph{both} of them.

The simulation strategy in this scenario is essentially a combination of the simulators in \Cref{app:hash-pw,app:hash-pk}. When $\adv$ decrypts $(s,T)$ under $\pw$, $\sim$ records $\langle\pw,pk,sk\rangle$ (although there might be a large number of such decryption attempts, and $\sim$ does not know which is the ``right'' one at this point). $\sim$ also computes $K'$ while simulating the $P'$ side. Later, when $\adv$ sends $(c^*,\tau')$, $\sim$ can tell if $\adv$ is performing the aforementioned attack by checking if there exists a record $\langle\pw,pk,sk\rangle$ that satisfies $\Decap(sk,c^*) = K'$ (using the specific example in \Cref{fig:attack-no-pw-pk}: When $\adv$ sends $(Y^2,\tau')$, $\sim$ finds the record $\langle\pw,X,x\rangle$ that satisfies $(Y^2)^x = K'$, where $K' = (X^2)^y$ was computed while encapsulating $X^2$ on $(s^*,T^*)$ from $\adv$). In this way $\sim$ can extract the password guess $\pw$ on the $P$ side.

We show the new simulator in \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-nothing}. (Only the steps after receiving $(c^*,\tau^*)$ are included.)

\begin{figure}[H]
\begin{framed}
\begin{small}
\underline{$P$'s session key}

\medskip
On $(c^*,\tau^*)$ from $\func$, simulate $P$'s output as follows:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item If $(s^*,T^*) = (s,T) \land (c^*,\tau^*) = (c,\tau')$, send $(\NewKey, sid, P', 0^n)$ to $\func$. \textcolor[gray]{0.5}{// $\adv$ is passive}
  {\color{blue}\item If $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land \tau^* = \tau'$, and there has been a $(\TestPwd, sid, P, \pw')$ command to $\func$ resulting in ``correct guess'' (i.e., $\sim$ previously entered step 2(b)(ii) in \Cref{fig:oeke-sim2}), there must be a $K'$ and an $SK'$ defined at that time.
      \begin{enumerate}
        \item Retrieve all records in the form of $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$. For each such record, compute $K := \Decap(sk^*,c^*)$ and check if $K^* = K'$. If there is more than one such record, abort. If there is no such record, go to step 3.
        \item If there is exactly one record $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ with $K := \Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K^*$, send $(\TestPwd, sid, P, \pw^*)$ to $\func$.
            \begin{enumerate}
              \item If $\func$ replies with ``wrong guess'', send $(\NewKey, sid, P, 0^n)$ to $\func$.
              \item If $\func$ replies with ``correct guess'', send $(\NewKey, sid, P, SK')$ to $\func$.
            \end{enumerate}
      \end{enumerate}
  }
  \item Otherwise find $\adv$'s {\color{blue}$\Htag(K^*)$} query resulting in $\tau^*$.
      \begin{enumerate}
        \item If there is no such query or more than one such query, send $(\TestPwd, sid, P, \bot)$ and then $(\NewKey, sid, P, 0^n)$ to $\func$.
        \item If there is exactly one such query, retrieve all records in the form of $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$. For each such record, compute $K := \Decap(sk^*,c^*)$ and check if $K^* = K$. If there is more than one such record, abort. If there is no such record, send $(\TestPwd, sid, P, \bot)$ and then $(\NewKey, sid, P, 0^n)$ to $\func$.
        \item If there is exactly one record $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ with $K := \Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K^*$, send $(\TestPwd, sid, P, \pw^*)$ to $\func$.
            \begin{enumerate}
              \item If $\func$ replies with ``wrong guess'', send $(\NewKey, sid, P, 0^n)$ to $\func$.
              \item If $\func$ replies with ``correct guess'', compute $SK := \Hkey(K)$ and send $(\NewKey, sid, P, SK)$ to $\func$.
            \end{enumerate}
      \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}
\end{small}
\end{framed}
\caption{UC simulator $\sim$ for the ``hash $pk$'' variant of OEKE. Only the paragraph that needs essential change from \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-st} (shown in {\color{blue}blue}) is included}
\label{fig:oeke-sim-hash-nothing}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection{Changes in Game Hops}
\label{app:game-hops-hash-nothing}
Once more, we only describe Game 2 and the four new games.

\medskip\noindent\emph{Game 2:} In the case where $(s^*,T^*) = (s,T) \land (\pw \neq \pw' \lor c^* \neq c)$, find $\adv$'s $\Htag(K^*)$ query resulting in $\tau^*$. If there is none or more than one, set $SK := \bot$. If there is a unique such query, do the following:
\begin{itemize}
  \item If $K^* = K$, set $SK := \Hkey(K)$ (i.e., there is no change from Game 1).
  \item If $K^* \neq K$, set $SK := \bot$.
\end{itemize}

Furthermore, in the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T)$, do the same as above, except that we now find $\adv$ \emph{or $P'$}'s $\Htag(K^*)$ query resulting in $\tau^*$.

The difference between the new Game 2 and the old one is that we now have two separate cases:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item $(s^*,T^*) = (s,T) \land (\pw \neq \pw' \lor c^* \neq c)$;
  \item $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T)$.
\end{enumerate}
These two cases combined yield $\neg(\pw = \pw' \land (s^*,T^*) = (s,T) \land c^* = c)$, which is identical to the case in the old Game 2 (i.e., they cover all situations other than the one addressed in Game 1). In case (1) we proceed as in the old Game 2, whereas in case (2) we cannot due to the ``attack'' in \Cref{fig:attack-no-pw-pk}; instead, in case (2) we need to include $P'$'s $\Htag$ query as if it were $\adv$'s.
\begin{claim}
\label{clm:htag8}
Suppose the CBIND and KBIND properties hold for the KEM scheme. In case (1) above, the probability that $P'$ queries $\Htag(K)$ is negligible.
\end{claim}
\begin{proof}
This has been proven in \Cref{clm:htag4} (most of the proof of \Cref{clm:htag4} assumes $(s^*,T^*) = (s,T)$, which is also in the condition of this game).
\end{proof}

\medskip
Games 3--16 are essentially unchanged from the corresponding games in \Cref{sec:hash-st}.

\medskip\noindent\emph{New game I:} Now that $(\pw')^*$ --- the password guess extracted from $(s^*,T^*)$ --- is defined (in Game 15), we can finally add (part of) what's in step 2 of \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-nothing}. In this game we first add the aborting condition. Concretely, in the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (\pw')^* = \pw' \land \tau^* = \tau'$, there must be a $K'$ defined on the $P'$ side. Retrieve all records in the form of $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$. If there is more than one such record such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, abort. If $(\pw')^*$ is undefined, treat it as if $(\pw')^* \neq \pw'$. (This corresponds to step 2(a) in the simulator in \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-nothing}.)

Note that in each such record, $(\star,sk^*) \gets \Gen$ is freshly generated, so a straightforward reduction to the CR property of the KEM scheme, $\red_\text{CR2}$, shows that the probability that the same $c^*$ decapsulates to the same $K^*$ under two freshly generated $sk^*$ is upper-bounded by $q_2 \cdot \Adv_{\red_\text{CR2}}^\text{CR}$ (cf. Game 13 in \Cref{sec:hash-pk-st-games}). We have
\[
\Dist_\env^{16,\text{newI}} \leq q_2 \cdot \Adv_{\red_\text{CR2}}^\text{CR}.
\]

\medskip\noindent\emph{New game II:} Again in the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (\pw')^* = \pw' \land \tau^* = \tau'$, if there is a unique record $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, and $\pw^* = \pw$, set $SK := SK'$. (In some sense this is the most crucial new game, as it covers the scenario in \Cref{fig:attack-no-pw-pk} --- which is why we need to make all these changes to begin with. It also corresponds to step 2(b)(ii) in the simulator in \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-nothing}. We will deal with step 2(b)(i) later.)

Since $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T)$, Game 16 finds $\adv$ or $P'$'s $\Htag$ query resulting in $\tau^*$. $P'$ makes a singe query to $\Htag$, on input $K'$; and the query result is $\tau' = \tau^*$. Therefore, Game 16 finds this query, and does the following:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Retrieve all records in the form of $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$, and check if there is such a (unique) record such that $K := \Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$. This is indeed the case per the condition of the game.
  \item Check if $\pw^* = \pw$, which is again the case per the condition of the game.
  \item Set $SK := \Hkey(K) (= \Hkey(K') = SK')$.
\end{enumerate}
This entire process in Game 16 eventually results in setting $SK := SK'$, which exactly matches the new game. Thus, there is no difference. We have
\[
\Dist_\env^{\text{newI},\text{newII}} = 0.
\]

\noindent\emph{New game III:} In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) $ (i.e., case (2) in Game 2), we find $\adv$ \emph{or $P'$}'s $\Htag(K^*)$ query resulting in $\tau^*$ --- except that in new Game II's condition, new Game II already shortcuts it to $SK := SK'$ without trying to find any $\Htag$ query. In this game, we do not check $P'$'s $\Htag$ query anymore, and only check $\adv$'s $\Htag$ query.

There are the following cases:
\begin{itemize}
  \item If $\tau^* \neq \tau'$, then of course $P'$'s $\Htag$ query does not result in $\tau^*$ (it results in $\tau'$), so we can safely drop $P'$'s $\Htag$ query.
  \item If $\tau^* = \tau'$, but there is more than one $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ record such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, then both new Game II and new Game III abort, regardless of $\Htag$ queries by $\adv$ or $P'$. Similarly, if $\tau^* = \tau'$, but there is no $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ record such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, then both new Game II and new Game III outright set $SK := \bot$. Thus, the two games are identical.
  \item If $(\pw')^* \neq \pw' \land \tau^* = \tau'$, then $P'$'s $\Htag(K')$ query results in $\tau^*$, and we need to argue that this query can be dropped from the check.
      \begin{itemize}
        \item If $\adv$ queries $\Htag(K')$, then new Game II still finds this query, so new Game II and new Game III are identical.
        \item If $\adv$ does not query $\Htag(K')$, then new Game III sets $SK := \bot$. We argue that new Game II also sets $SK := \bot$ except with negligible probability. New Game II finds $P'$'s $\Htag(K')$ query, then finds the (unique) $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ record such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, and sets $SK := \bot$ unless $\pw^* = \pw$. We claim that $\pw^* = \pw$, i.e., there is a $\langle\pw,pk,sk\rangle$ record such that $\Decap(sk,c^*) = K'$, with negligible probability. $pk$ and $pk'$ are independent of each other by \Cref{clm:pk-ind}, so a reduction to the KBIND property of the KEM scheme similar to that in new Game II of \Cref{app:game-hops-hash-pw}, $\red_\text{KBIND2}$, shows that $\Pr[\pw^* = \pw] \leq (q_2+1)\Adv_{\red_\text{KBIND2}}^\text{KBIND}$.
      \end{itemize}
  \item If $(\pw')^* = \pw' \land \tau^* = \tau'$, there is a unique $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ record such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, but $\pw^* \neq \pw$, then again both new Game II and new Game III outright set $SK := \bot$, regardless of $\Htag$ queries by $\adv$ or $P'$. Thus, the two games are identical.
\end{itemize}

The remaining case is that $(\pw')^* = \pw' \land \tau^* = \tau'$, there is a unique $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ record such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, and $\pw^* = \pw$. The condition of the game additionally requires $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T)$. Combined, we get ``$(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (\pw')^* = \pw' \land \tau^* = \tau'$, there is a unique $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ record such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, and $\pw^* = \pw$''. This is exactly the condition in new Game II, in which case we do not make any change in new Game III. Overall, we conclude that
\[
\Dist_\env^{\text{newII},\text{newIII}} \leq (q_2+1)\Adv_{\red_\text{KBIND2}}^\text{KBIND}.
\]

Game 17 is essentially unchanged from the corresponding game in \Cref{sec:hash-pw-st}.

\medskip\noindent\emph{New Game IV:} In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land \tau^* = \tau'$, if there is a unique record $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, but $\pw^* \neq \pw$, set $SK := \bot$. (This corresponds to step 2(c)(i) in \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-nothing}.)

In this case Game 17 first tries to find $\adv$'s (unique) $\Htag(K^*)$ query resulting in $\tau^*$. Since $\tau^* = \tau' = \Htag(K')$, if $\adv$ does not query $\Htag(K')$, then Game 17 sets $SK := \bot$. Otherwise Game 17 finds $\adv$'s $\Htag(K')$ query, and then tries to find the (unique) $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ record such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, and checks if $\pw^* = \pw$ (see Game 13). Since $\pw^* \neq \pw$, the check never passes, so Game 17 again sets $SK := \bot$. Either way, Game 17 matches new Game IV which outright sets $SK := \bot$. We have
\[
\Dist_\env^{17,\text{newIV}} = 0.
\]

\paragraph{Summary.}
We have made the following essential changes to the games:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T)$, Game 2 tries to find $P'$'s $\Htag$ query resulting in $\tau^*$ (in addition to $\adv$'s query).
  \item In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land (\pw')^* = \pw' \land \tau^* = \tau'$, and there is more than one record $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, new Game I aborts.
  \item In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land \tau^* = \tau'$, there is a unique record $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, and $\pw^* = \pw$, new Game II outright sets $SK := SK'$ (without trying to find any $\Htag$ query by $\adv$ or $P'$).
  \item In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T)$, new Game III drops the check on $P'$'s $\Htag$ query introduced in Game 2 (except in new Game II's condition, where all checks on $\Htag$ queries have already been dropped).
  \item In the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land \tau^* = \tau'$, there is a unique record $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$, and $\pw^* \neq \pw$, new Game IV outright sets $SK := \bot$.
\end{enumerate}

Items (1) and (4) introduce the check on $P'$'s $\Htag$ query and then drop it, so there is no net change there. To see the net change, we only need to combine items (2), (3) and (5), which say that in the case where $(s^*,T^*) \neq (s,T) \land \tau^* = \tau'$ and there is a record $\langle\pw^*,\star,sk^*\rangle$ such that $\Decap(sk^*,c^*) = K'$,
\begin{itemize}
  \item If the record is not unique, then the game aborts [item (2)].
  \item If the record is unique, and $\pw^* = \pw$, then we set $SK := SK'$ [item (3)].
  \item If the record is unique, and $\pw^* \neq \pw$, then we set $SK := \bot$ [item (5)].
\end{itemize}
This exactly matches step 2 of the simulator in \Cref{fig:oeke-sim-hash-nothing}, so new Game IV is identical to the ideal world. This completes the proof.

\section{Comparison with \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25}}
\label{app:jrx-comparison}
We believe our security proof for the ``hashing nothing but $K$'' in \Cref{app:hash-nothing} is fundamentally similar to the OEKE security proof in \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25}; however, the structure and presentation of their proof is drastically different from ours. Below we give a high-level comparison with \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25}, although a game-by-game comparison --- like what we did in \Cref{sec:games} --- seems impossible.

\paragraph{Modular structure of \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25}.}
While our proof is a monolith, the proof in \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} is split into three levels:
\begin{itemize}
  \item \cite[Section~4]{EC:JanRoyXu25} gives an ideal functionality $\func_\text{POPF}$ for the 2-round Feistel cipher. In fact the 2F construction does not appear to UC-realize $\func_\text{POPF}$ (or any reasonable UC functionality); to circumvent this, \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} only proves that 2F ``almost'' UC-realizes $\func_\text{POPF}$, a relaxation of UC which places some restrictions on the upper-level protocol composed with 2F. We refer to \cite[Section~4]{EC:JanRoyXu25} for more details.
  \item \cite[Section~5.1]{EC:JanRoyXu25} proves that the $P$-to-$P'$ message, using $\func_\text{POPF}$, UC-realizes a ``first-round'' functionality $\func_\text{EKE-1r}$. The reason why they do this is that the $P$-to-$P'$ message in EKE and OEKE is exactly identical, so having this common first-round functionality avoids copying a large chunk of argument across two security proofs. However, if we only care about OEKE then this abstraction is unnecessary.
  \item Finally, \cite[Section~5.3]{EC:JanRoyXu25} proves that OEKE (the ``hash nothing but $K$'' version), using $\func_\text{EKE-1r}$, UC-realizes the standard PAKE functionality $\func_\text{PAKE}$. (They do not consider the PAKE functionality with initiator-side explicit authentication.)
\end{itemize}
If we collect and combine all proofs in the three sections of \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} using the composition theorem, that should yield a monolith security proof for OEKE. However, there would be so much work that we might as well perform a monolith proof from scratch --- as what we did.

\paragraph{KEM scheme: hashed v. unhashed.}
Another difficulty when comparing our security proof with the one in \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} is that they put the ROs $\Hkey$ and $\Htag$ inside the KEM scheme.\footnote{In fact, \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} uses the syntax of (unauthenticated) key exchange protocols, rather than KEM.} In our description of OEKE, we use the KEM scheme to derive the KEM key $K$, and then use $\Hkey$ and $\Htag$ to derive the PAKE session key $SK$ and the authenticator $\tau'$. In contrast, \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} splits the KEM key as $SK||\tau'$, without applying an RO --- which means that the ROs are implicitly ``baked into'' the KEM scheme.

In more detail, let $(\Gen,\Encap,\Decap)$ be a KEM scheme as used in our description of OEKE. We can construct another KEM scheme $(\Gen,\Encap',\Decap')$ as follows:
\begin{itemize}
  \item $\Encap'(pk)$: Run $(c,K) \gets \Encap(pk)$, compute $SK := \Hkey(K)$ and $\tau' := \Htag(K)$, and output $(c,SK||\tau')$.
  \item $\Decap'(sk,c)$: Run $K \gets \Decap(pk)$, compute $SK := \Hkey(K)$ and $\tau' := \Htag(K)$, and output $SK||\tau'$.
\end{itemize}
Then on the PAKE level, the OEKE scheme can use the ``raw'' KEM key as $SK$ and $\tau'$, without mentioning $\Hkey$ or $\Htag$.

Outputting the ``raw'' KEM key also means that \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} needs stronger KEM security properties than ours. The OEKE security result in \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} requires three KEM security properties: first pseudorandomness, pseudorandom non-malleability, and collision resistance. First pseudorandomness is the same as what we call UNI-PK. Pseudorandom non-malleability roughly corresponds to IND-1CCA\$, whereas we only need OW-1PCA and ANO-1PCA. (It shouldn't be surprising that they need IND and we only need OW: they use the ``raw'' KEM key as the PAKE session key, whereas we use an RO of the KEM key --- so we only need the KEM key to be unpredictable given the KEM ciphertext.) Collision resistance is stronger than our CR, and roughly corresponds to the combination of the additional properties in \Cref{sec:kem-additional}.

\begin{remark}
\cite[Section~1]{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} correctly observes that \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} requires stronger KEM security properties, but does not specify \emph{why}. There are, in fact, two orthogonal reasons:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} puts the ROs inside the KEM scheme, so it needs ``indistinguishability'' rather than ``unprecitability'' type of properties;
  \item \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} only hashes $K$ in $\Htag$, whereas \cite{EPRINT:ArrBarJar25} hashes everything.
\end{enumerate}
(1) explains why \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} needs pseudorandom non-malleability, and (2) explains why \cite{EC:JanRoyXu25} needs collision resistance.
\end{remark}

\section{Deferred Proofs from \Cref{sec:prelim}}
\subsection{Proof of \Cref{lem:uni-ano-ro}}
\label{app:uni-ano-ro-proof}
We need two helper lemmas first.
\begin{lemma}
\label{lem:uni-ow}
Suppose the UNI-PK and OW properties hold for a KEM scheme. Then for any PPT adversary $\adv$, the probability that $\adv$ wins the following game is negligible:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    $pk \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,K) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $K^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$ \\
    $\adv$ wins if $K^* = K$
    }
\]
(Below we call this property UNI-OW.)
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
This is straightforward so we only provide a sketch. We claim that the following two games are indistinguishable:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{UNI-OW game}: \\
    $pk \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,K) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $K^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$ \\
    $\adv$ wins if $K^* = K$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{OW game}: \\
    $(pk,\star) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(c,K) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $K^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$ \\
    $\adv$ wins if $K^* = K$
    }
\]
This is due to a reduction $\red_\text{UNI-PK}$ to the UNI-PK property of the KEM scheme: $\red_\text{UNI-PK}$, on input $pk$, can run either the UNI-OW game or the OW-game --- depending on whether its challenge bit $b = 1$ (i.e., $pk$ is uniformly random) or $b = 0$ (i.e., $pk$ is honestly generated) --- and output 1 if $\adv$ wins and 0 otherwise. The difference between $\Pr[\adv\text{ wins the UNI-OW game}]$ and $\Pr[\adv\text{ wins the OW game}]$ is equal to $\red_\text{UNI-PK}$'s advantage, which must be negligible. Since the OW property holds for the KEM scheme, $\Pr[\adv\text{ wins the OW}\allowbreak\text{game}]$ is also negligible. So $\Pr[\adv\text{ wins the UNI-OW game}]$ must be negligible.
\end{proof}
\begin{lemma}
\label{lem:uni-ano}
Suppose the UNI-PK and ANO properties hold for a KEM scheme. Then for any PPT adversary $\adv$, $\Pr[b^* = 1]$ in the following two games are negligibly close:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 0$}: \\
    $pk \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 1$}: \\
    $pk,pk' \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk')$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$
    }
\]
(Below we call this property UNI-ANO.)
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
The proof goes by a sequence of games, and we again only provide a sketch. The following two games
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 0$}: \\
    $pk \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 0$ in the ANO game}: \\
    $(pk,\star) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$
    }
\]
are indistinguishable due to the UNI-PK property of the KEM scheme (the reduction is very similar to that in the proof of \Cref{lem:uni-ow}, except that here the reduction copies $\adv$'s output bit instead of making a guess and outputting one of $\adv$'s RO queries); the following two games
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 0$ in the ANO game}: \\
    $(pk,\star) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 1$ in the ANO game}: \\
    $(pk,\star) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(pk',\star) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk')$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$
    }
\]
are indistinguishable because this is exactly what the ANO property of the KEM scheme says; finally, the following two games
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 1$ in the ANO game}: \\
    $(pk,\star) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(pk',\star) \gets \Gen$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk')$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 1$}: \\
    $pk,pk' \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk')$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c)$
    }
\]
are indistinguishable again due to the UNI-PK property of the KEM scheme.
\end{proof}
\begin{proof}[Proof of \Cref{lem:uni-ano-ro}]
The proof goes by a sequence of games, and we again only provide a sketch. Suppose $\adv$ makes $q_1,q_2$ queries to ROs $H_1,H_2$, respectively. We start from the game where the challenge bit $b = 0$. To begin with, we claim that $\Pr[b^* = 1]$ does not change if we simplify the game so that $\adv$ cannot choose its index $i$:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 0$}: \\
    $pk_1, \dots, pk_q \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $i \gets \adv(pk_1, \dots, pk_q)$ \\
    $(c,K) \gets \Encap(pk_i)$ \\
    $h_1 := H_1(K); h_2 := H_2(K)$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(c,h_1,h_2)$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{simplified $b = 0$ game}: \\
    $pk \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $h_1 := H_1(K); h_2 := H_2(K)$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c,h_1,h_2)$
    }
\]
Note that no matter which index $i$ $\adv$ chooses, once $i$ is fixed $\adv$'s view is identical to its view in the simplified game (plus redundant public keys $pk_{i^*}$ for $i^* \neq i$, which are not used anywhere in the game and thus do not affect $\Pr[b^* = 1]$). Therefore, letting
\begin{align*}
p_{i^*} &= \Pr[b^* = 1 \mid i = i^*\text{ in the original game}], \\
p &= \Pr[b^* = 1\text{ in the simplified game}],
\end{align*}
we have that for any $i^* \in [q]$,
\[
p_{i^*} = p.
\]
But then
\begin{align*}
\Pr[b^* = 1 \text{ in the original game}] &= \sum_{i^* = 1}^q p_{i^*}\cdot\Pr[i = i^*] \\
&= \sum_{i^* = 1}^q p\cdot\Pr[i = i^*] \\
&= p\cdot\sum_{i^* = 1}^q \Pr[i = i^*] = p,
\end{align*}
so $\Pr[b^* = 1]$ in the original game and the simplified game are equal. (Note that we do not need a hybrid argument here.)

Next, we sample $h_1,h_2$ at random rather than set them to the real RO outputs:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{simplified $b = 0$ game}: \\
    $pk \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $h_1 := H_1(K); h_2 := H_2(K)$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c,h_1,h_2)$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{intermediate game}: \\
    $pk \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,K) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $h_1 \gets \{0,1\}^n; h_2 \gets \{0,1\}^{2n}$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c,h_1,h_2)$
    }
\]

The two games are identical unless $\adv$ queries either $H_1(K)$ or $H_2(K)$, which happens with negligible probability due to a reduction $\red_\text{UNI-OW}$ to the UNI-OW property of the KEM scheme (\Cref{lem:uni-ow}). $\red_\text{UNI-OW}$, on input $(pk,c)$, samples $i \gets [q_1+q_2]$ as a guess that $\adv$'s $i$-th $H_1$ or $H_2$ query is on $K$. Then $\red_\text{UNI-OW}$ samples $pk \gets \pkspace, h_1 \gets \{0,1\}^n, h_2 \gets \{0,1\}^{2n}$ and invokes $\adv$ on input $(pk,c,h_1,h_2)$ to $\adv$. On $\adv$'s $i$-th $H_1$ or $H_2$ query (say the query input is $K^*$), $\red_\text{UNI-OW}$ outputs $K^*$. Assuming $\adv$ queries either $H_1(K)$ or $H_2(K)$, and $\red_\text{UNI-OW}$'s guess of index $i$ is correct, $\red_\text{UNI-OW}$ simulates the two games perfectly until the ``bad event'' happens and wins the UNI-OW game. So the above two games are indistinguishable (with a $q_1+q_2$ reduction loss).

After that, we change the generation of $c$ from using $pk_i$ to using a fresh $pk'$:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{intermediate game}: \\
    $pk \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk)$ \\
    $h_1 \gets \{0,1\}^n; h_2 \gets \{0,1\}^{2n}$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c,h_1,h_2)$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{simplified $b = 1$ game}: \\
    $pk,pk' \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk')$ \\
    $h_1 \gets \{0,1\}^n; h_2 \gets \{0,1\}^{2n}$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c,h_1,h_2)$
    }
\]
The two games are indistinguishable, due to the UNI-ANO property of the KEM scheme (\Cref{lem:uni-ano}). (The only difference is that here $\adv$ additionally sees $h_1,h_2$, which are not used anywhere in the game.)

Finally, we bring back $\adv$'s ability to choose an index $i$ and arrive at the game where the challenge bit $b = 1$:
\[
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{simplified $b = 1$ game}: \\
    $pk,pk' \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk')$ \\
    $h_1 \gets \{0,1\}^n; h_2 \gets \{0,1\}^{2n}$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(pk,c,h_1,h_2)$
    }
    \quad
    \fcodebox{
    \underline{challenge bit $b = 1$}: \\
    $pk',pk_1, \dots, pk_q \gets \pkspace$ \\
    $i \gets \adv(pk_1, \dots, pk_q)$ \\
    $(c,\star) \gets \Encap(pk')$ \\
    $h_1 \gets \{0,1\}^n; h_2 \gets \{0,1\}^{2n}$ \\
    $b^* \gets \adv(c,h_1,h_2)$
    }
\]
An argument similar to the first game hop shows that $\Pr[b^* = 1]$ in the two games above are equal, completing the proof.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Proof of \Cref{lem:kbind-eq}}
\label{app:kbind-eq-proof}
\begin{proof}
We only provide a sketch. First,
\[
      \operatorname{Pr}
      \left[
      \begin{array}{c}
      K = K'
      \end{array}
      :
      \begin{array}{c}
      (pk,sk) \gets \Gen \\
      pk' \gets \pkspace \\
      (c,K') \gets \Encap(pk') \\
      K := \Decap(sk,c)
      \end{array}
      \right]
\]
and
\begin{equation}
\label{eq:kbind-eq-real}
      \operatorname{Pr}
      \left[
      \begin{array}{c}
      K = K'
      \end{array}
      :
      \begin{array}{c}
      (pk,sk) \gets \Gen \\
      (pk',\star) \gets \Gen \\
      (c,K') \gets \Encap(pk') \\
      K := \Decap(sk,c)
      \end{array}
      \right]
\tag{\textdagger}
\end{equation}
are negligibly close due to a reduction $\red$ to the UNI-PK property of the KEM scheme: $\red$, on input $pk'$, computes $(pk,sk) \gets \Gen$, $(c,K') \gets \Encap(pk')$, and $K := \Decap(sk,c)$, and outputs 1 if $K = K'$ and 0 otherwise.

Next, we show \eqref{eq:kbind-eq-real} is negligible. Consider the following adversary $\adv$ in the OW game for the KEM scheme: $\adv$, on input $(pk',c)$, computes $(pk,sk) \gets \Gen$ and $K := \Decap(sk,c)$, and outputs $K$. $\adv$ wins the game if and only if the equation in \eqref{eq:kbind-eq-real} holds, so
\[
\eqref{eq:kbind-eq-real} = \Adv_{\adv}^\text{OW},
\]
which is negligible.
\end{proof}
